limited.
Sixth Report op the
Directors.
The result of the six months’ business to 31st March last is now presented to
the shareholders.
The net profit, less expenses of management, interest paid and accrued on
deposits, rebate on current bills, &c.,
Amounted to . . . . ..
The balance from previous half-year was ..
..£13,656 5 10
.. 9,558 6 1
Making a total of
Deduct note-tax paid to Australian Governments and
income-tax in Great Britain ..
Leaving available .. .. ..
Carry forward to next half-year
The total paid-up capital of the bank now stands at £1,865,930 19s. 9d., being
an increase of £44,209 19s. during the half-year. ,
The directors have given notice to the deferred deposit receipt holders of their
intention to pay off befoi'e maturity one-fifth of the liabilities of the bank in that
form. The operation has already commenced, and will be completed next month in
berms of the notice.’
The Ordinary General Meeting will be held on Wednesday, 6th May, at noon,
at the head office, Collins-street, Melbourne.
In conformity with the articles of association, Mr. John Grice and Mr. Arthur
Septimus King retire from the board on that occasion. These gentlemen both offer
themselves for re-election. There are no other candidates.
The shareholders will also be called on to appoint two auditors for the year and
bo fix their remuneration. The gentleman now acting in that capacity are Mr.
W. H. Tuckett and Mr. Andrew Burns.
By order of the board of directors.
P. G. SMITH, Chief Manager.
The National Bank of Australasia Limited,
Head Office, Melbourne, 27th April, 1896.

and that in our opinion the balance-sheet is a full and fair balance-sheet, containing the particulars required by the

THE OBJECTS FOR WHICH THE COMPANY IS TO BE FORMED ARE AS FOLLOWS
L —To acquire by purchase the land situated at CROYDON, QUEENSLAND, being the whole of
the Gold mining Property known as the KING OF CROYDON NO. 1 NORTH AND
P. C. AMALGAMATED GOLD-MINING CLAIMS, and held under Gold-mining Lease
No. 617. 2.—To acquire (if desirable) other valuable ground adjoining that comprised in the
said lease. 3.—To explore, work, and mine all properties to be acquired by the Company.
The Vendors agree to sell to the Company the said claims for 30,000 shares fully paid up. The
document, dated 12th March, 1896, containing the conditions upon which the Vendors agree to sell
may be inspected at the office of the Broker.
The only charges to be made will be the initial expense of floating and registering the Company.
The property to be acquired is 10 acres in area (which can be increased, if necessary, to 16 or 20
acres), and is situated about six miles from the township of Croydon. Both the claims are the very
pick of the King line, and are amongst the largest producers of gold in that line, and have been worked
for the last 10 years. The want of capital to develop them systematically has been the only obstacle
to retard their progress. The following returns, taken from the Warden’s Office, Croydon, show the
arushings up to the present time :
Tons. cwt. Oz. dwt. gr.
King of Croydon No. 1 North 784 0 ... 3477 10 0
~ ~ P.C. Amalgamated ... 532 *3 ... 2334 9 12
The ore is not of a refractory nature, as the present owners sent 16 tons of stone obtained from
these claims to N. Parker and Company, Footscray Works, Melbourne, for which they paid the
owners £l5 per ton, and that firm informed them the best mode of treatment for the ore was the
ordinary stamper battery, and plenty pf grinding in the Berdan pans.
The present owners also, in January last, had forty (40) tons of surface soil well screened, and
then crushed and obtained a yield of to the ton. There is a large quantity of this class of soil
upon the giound, and with a battery belonging to the Company it should pay well. The owners of
the claim adjoining are crushing similar surface soil with their own battery, and make 4Jdwt. to the
ton pay.
The proposed mode of working (should the Company be formed) is as follows :—lt will be
noticed, on reference to the plan, that a proposed new underlie shaft is shown thereon. With this
new shaft, which (to reach the point wished to be got at, viz., the deep block shaft) will need to go
3G9ft. odd, and will be in reef country the whole way, with the certainty of getting the good stone
which has been worked in shaft marked “ A.” This stone should be cut in the new underlie shaft at
about 120 ft., and is the reef from which the last crushing that went B£oz. to the ton was taken, and
this reef remains even richer than when the last crushing was taken out. Then, again, in regard to
the proposed underlie shaft, there is every possibility of stacking good stone from the start, as the
shaft will commence from the cap of the reef. It is proposed to let a contract to sink the underlie
shaft, say, 200 ft.
At the commencement, up to the first 120 ft. or 200 ft., an engine and pump could be hired at Croy-
don for about £3 or £3 10s. per week, but after that distance is reached it would be advisable to buy a
good engine and pump.
The following paragraphs, under the head of “ Mining Intelligence,” appeared in the issue of the
Croydon Golden Age of the 21st February, 1896 : —“ The Glencoe Gold-mining and Recovery Com-
pany has been registered in Melbourne as a No Liability Company, with a capital of £40,000, divided
into 40,000 shares of .£1 each ;of these, 24,000 are fully,paid up, and go to the vendors. The Krupp
ball dry crusher for the Glencoe mine is en board the Duke of Argyll, which sailed from London at
the beginning of January, and should, therefore, arrive at Thursday Island at the end of February.
The cyanide vats will be shipped by the next boat from the South by Rodney Brothers, of Townsville.”
This mine is in the immediate neighborhood of the claims to be acquired by the Company, as also
is the “ Waratah,” a mine which was recently floated in England ; and a late telegram from Croydon
states that rich stone has been struck at depth of over 500 ft. on the underlie shaft.
Sydney, 27th April. 1806.

BEFORE YOU
T. B A q i
When you go out shopping, consider, think
of the money in your pocket, and take account
of the many stores whose various attractions
are laid before you. There is A, with temp-
tation in the form of a pretty show ; there is
B, whose wiles are innumerable ; and C,
who says that he is the man for your money ;
and so on, throughout a list as long as
George-street, each smiling dealer has some-
thing to say and to offer.
Please Pause!
Hearken not to the song of the syren, sing
she ever so sweetly ; heed not the voice of
the charmer, charm he never so wisely.
Your pocket’s concerned ; and there are
pirates about. As the poet pertinently puts
it: “ There be land-sharks and water-
sharks,” and it is for you to see that your
dealings are with none of these voracious
monsters. Take warning in time, for it will
be no use when the new taxes come round—
and the tax-gatherer won’t take no for an
answer—for you to plead : There would have
been enough and to spare had I been wise
enough to

Gig Fours .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You can see their rudder hissing
1 ast your canvas, and the kissing
1 the wash that fans behind them on your bow-
side makes her roll;
And your coxswain’s calling “Quicker ! ”
As your breath comes thick and thicker,
xSut you put in half-a-dozen fit to bust your blessed
soul.
And the boat is leaking badly,
And you fling your weight on madly;
.nut your stick has got no packing and it runs
down every dip;
And your instep’s sore and skinning,
Still you keep a hope of winning
I ill the wind flips on your oar-blade and you bend
your stretcher-clip.
Every man is outward swinging,
And a dull and sullen singing
Is vibrating in your ear-drums as you feel them
screw and squirm;
And, through your faultless hauling,
You can hear their youngster calling
That you’re too almighty slow to catch a paralytic
worm.
Then there sounds a scraping under
And your seat and slide’s asunder ;
While you’re spitting chips like thunder and your
knees are fairly broke ;
And the streams of sweat near blind you
As you damn the mugs behind you ;
Or, if you’re three or two or bow, you blame the
rotten stroke.
Then you strike the grip and feather;
Whip a few in hell-for-leather,
And you’re swinging all together just like clock-
work for a spell—
Then the wind comes round an angle
And your form gets in a tangle,
And you’re chucking up the water and the time is
all to hell.
Then you see their wash runs stronger -
And decide to hang out longer,
Till at last amid the swirling pools their rudder
starts to peep ;
And your heart grows big and bigger
As you spot their coxswain’s figure,
And inch by inch along their side you slowly crawl
and creep.
Then you think how far the boat is
Erom the winning-post; no notice
You take of form or feeling, time or troubles,
needs or knack;
With the swaying, swinging, sweeping
Of the oars you feel her leaping,
And you feel the muscles swelling on your
shoulders and your back.
For their stroke looks done ; the catch he
Sets is slow and short and scratchy,
And their bow has cracked up badly; he’s a deader
to the world;
Ho ! their barrackers yell louder,
And they graft like nigs to crowd her,
But there’s nothing left but arm-work in them :
all their backs are curled.
Loud and louder grow the raging
Cries that echo from the staging,
And you pop on half-a-dozen with all your skill
and strength;
Then, with resting arms before you,
Rings the judge’s pistol o’er you,
And you’ve done them like a dinner by an easy
half-a-length.
Melb.
MONTAGUE GROVER.
Mr. Justice Dowling, a former N.S.W.
Chief-Justice, once sentenced a man, convicted of
stealing a pair of oars, to one month’s gaol, and,
at the end of that period, to be flogged at the
cart’s tail from the police-station to the wharf in
King-st.
In a Melb. restaurant, the other night, a
certain flash young jock condescended to chaff his
two middle-aged companions, one of whom had
“ shouted ” the dinner. “ Look at me, and kick
yourselves,” said the sarcastic lightweight, “ sweet
twenty, dear boys, with all my life before me.
Why, you’ve been through all the fun you talk
about, but mine, mine is only just coming on.”
The grey-haired sinner seated opposite nodded his
head scornfully, put his elbows on the table,
peered into the hairless countenance of the sweet
twenty-year-old, and snarled, “You’ll never live
to go through it.” A fair answer. What said
the Spectator, of August 25th, 1711? “The
youth, indeed, hopes for many more days, so can-
not the old man. The youth’s hopes are ill-
grounded, for what is more foolish than to place
any confidence upon an uncertainty ? But the old
man has not room so much as to hope ; he is still
happier than the youth, he has already enjoyed
what the other does but hope for. One wishes to
live long, the other has lived long.” And the
Spectator was thinking of ordinary youths, not
of champagne-swilling boys, who daren’t put on a
decent layer of flesh to protect ’em from the weather.
Henry Parkes, at 82, contemplating a jockey
in the second stage of intoxication, would make a
fine moral subject for a great artist. Title, “Do
you think you’ll live to go through it ? ”
A man advertises, in a Melb. paper, for a
billet as domestic servant.
A fourth theatrical divorce-case has been
booked for the coming Sydney oourt.

SATURDAY, MAY 9, 1896.

A PREMIER’S PROGRESS.
"Ministers have been banqueting and speech-making, and receiving deputations, from the Queen laml lnorlor to Broken-hill, and people every part of
the country have had opportunities of personally impressing their local necessities on members of the Govermment, It is not to be wondered at that these chances
have been eagerly taken. And the residents would neglect their opportunities-in addition to depriving the Ministerial tour of its raison d’etre- if they did not bring
their axes to the grindstone so providentially sent.”— REID ORGAN.

SATURDAY, MAY 9, 1896.
A Woman and an Ass.
The South Australian election, now practi-
cally completed, is the first held on this con-
tinent in which the woman-voter has been a
factor, and her presence has served to make
the contest more than usually interesting.
The result has upset many calculations, and
turned much elaborate prevision into mock-
ery. When the great aggregate woman first
took a hand in Maoriland politics, the con-
sequence was so wholly unlike what was
generally expected that it was commonly set
down as a remarkable exception to an other-
wise reliable rule ; the repetition of the same
experience in S.A. creates a doubt whether
the exception isn’t really the rule and the
alleged rule itself a delusion. For many
years it has been the custom to regard woman
as the buttress of Conservatism and the
main support of priestcraft —as a being
whose irruption into politics would mean
retrogression, Toryism, the re-establish-
ment of clerical influence in educational
matters, and a general going back to
the old ideals. Woman, it was supposed,
was several centuries behind man, in a poli-
tical sense, and accordingly when John
Hall, the narrowest and most hidebound
Tory of his country and generation, first
seriously advocated the female suffrage in
Maoriland, he did it with the conviction that
the woman vote would help him to get back
to his own ideals, which were those of the
Middle Ages, or thereabouts. Now the new
order of things has had two separate trials
in two widely-removed provinces, and thus
far the result has proved either that woman
isn’t so far behind as was supposed, or else
that man isn’t so far advanced as he has de-
clared himself, on his own unsupported
authority, to be. So far as can be ascer-
tained the woman vote, both in Maoriland
and S.A., has gone quite as solidly for the
Democratic cause as that of the male voter,
and there has been no retrogression, no
upheaval of the clerical influence, no great
National Ass victory, and none of the other
things that were confidently anticipated.
* * * *
The S.A. Government appealed to the
country on two distinct issues. It asked for
a general approval of the Kingston Ministry
and its Democratic policy, and it called for a
popular vote on the question of maintaining
the present system of secular education or
reverting to denominationalisin. On this
second issue it was believed in many quarters
that the female vote would give a substantial
majority to the clerical interest. It is com-
monly assumed that woman is the main sup-
port of the Church ; that she forms the
largest half of the congregation in the white-
washed tabernacle where the priest drones
out his Sunday platitudes ; and that she has
a capacity for believing the aged story of
Jonah and other old and tough statements,
and a faculty for taking her creed seriously,
which are absent in the other sex. This
theory rested, in part, upon the census-paper
wherein the male citizen describes himself as
a “freethinker” or an “agnostic” much
more frequently than the female, but this
may have merely implied that the male
takes his atheism seriously and the female
doesn’t. Otherwise it rested mainly on
legend; it was one of these statements built on
the assertion of somebody that nobody ever
definitely heard of, which survives for want of
any positive evidence either one way or
another. The S.A. election has, however,
brought it into serious discredit. ‘ ‘ The
godless system of education ” came out on
top by an overwhelming majority. The
woman elector has reported that she doesn’t
want denominationalism at any price, and it
would seem that the she-Australian believes
in the clerical fetish about as much as the
he, or perhaps a little more, but at all events
not enough to make her faith a matter worth
mentioning.
The woman suffrage has done one or two
other things which were not expected of it,
and it has left undone various things which
certainly were expected. The Kingston
Government—the most distinctly democratic
Ministry S.A. has yet possessed was
strengthened instead of being overthrown.
The Labor wing of the Government party
was strengthened even more than the direct
Ministerial following, so the present Assem-
bly is more aggressively liberal than its prede-
cessor. In the frantic kaleidoscope of S.A.
politics it is very rarely indeed that a Govern-
ment has survived a whole Parliament as the
Kingston Ministry has done. The preceding
Parliament (the thirteenth in S.A. history)
saw three Ministries live and die. The
eighth Parliament survived four separate
Ministries. The seventh Parliament saw
three Cabinets come and go, and leave
hardly a ripple on the political surface. The
fourth Parliament, during its three years’
existence, killed off four Cabinets and badly
crippled a fifth. The third Parliament also
killed off four ephemeral Governments and
left a fifth in a state of ruin and disruption.
The second saw three Governments rise and
fall; and the first one wrecked three Minis-
tries also, while another Ministry perished
during the elections which followed.
When a S.A. Government has come safely
through an election without dying of the
process it has invariably returned with no
majority worth mentioning, and only lived
afterwards as a matter of form. According
to all the precedents of the province the
Kingston Ministry’s case was hopeless from
sheer old age, because it has already lived
longer than any other Cabinet in S.A.
It was alleged to be doubly hopeless
because, in addition to the old electors, who
were tired of it in the natural course
of things, it had to reckon with a vast mass
of new electors who were supposed to be
constitutionally'jrostile to it because it was
a Democratic and “Godless” administra-
tion. And, in the facp of all these prognos-
tications, Kingston and his colleagues have
come back with, to all appearance, a stronger
following than they had three years ago —a
thing which never happened before in the
quicksand of political life, as it is, or used to
be, at the Holy City. Such a result as this
breaks up the old theories and the old
National Ass, and leaves everything in a
state of uncertainty and bewilderment.
* * * *
Further, there was no great outbreak of
faddism, as was commonly anticipated. The
alleged Shrieking Sisterhood didn’t shriek to
any extent worth mentioning. The Cold Tea
element didn’t have a great day out; in fact,
it seems to have been rather less in evidence
than usual. The Social Purity Society,
which raged so fearfully in the City of
Churches nine or ten years ago, and has
raged more or less ever since, was so much
swamped in the general Democratic move-
ment that it could hardly be said to be
seriously in evidence. The Age of Consent
was talked about apparently no more than
usual, and the divided skirt was seen
just about as much as usual. The woman
voter didn’t blunder over her ballot-paper
much more than the average male. The
hysterical she-politician with her hair a-
hanging down her back, who was to rave
through the length and breadth of the land,
while a menial at home held the infant
upside-down with one hand, and read about
the impossible “ jook ” in the London Jour-
nal with the other, didn’t rave to any notice-
able extent, and her hair was much as it
generally is, and her strangled infant, so
far, hasn’t been heard about. The parson
appealed to the female element of his flock
m vain : either the woman doesn’t go to
church any more than the man, or else she
goes for amusement, or to exhibit her bon-
net, or else she keeps religion and business
strictly separate. ‘ In short, nothing hap-
pened as it was expected to happen, and
everything was upside down, unexpected and
unorthodox. The only visible result so far
is that the democratic vote is distinctly
stronger, and the Natio te^fall m
with a g r eat fall _, and than that
greater stability m k.A. ,P
province has yet exhibitec #
The woman suffrage was opposed injany
quarters on two grounds . ■ >
would always vote from mere force otJaM
the way her husband or her fathe
second) that she would always vote jrom
pure cussedness, the way hei h• < two
father didn’t vote It was also. the
more grounds : one, that sn naturally
Conservative ticket because she was ' y
Conservative, and the other tha ■

“Within an Ace of Greatness.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Henry Parkes is dead. For a generation past
he has been a Problem : henceforth he will be a
Legend. When the historian of the future picks
with his hammer-point against the rocks of a long-
dead past, he will wonder what manner of man it
was that so impressed the strata of this epoch.
For certain it is that he who now lies in his
quiet grave at Faulconbridge~has permanently
stamped his individuality upon the formative
period of Australian constitutionalism. Many
abler men, nobler men, truer patriots, many souls
more worthy of enduring honor and of that affec-
tionate remembrance which constitutes the splen-
did immortality of genius, will leave upon the
drift and sands deposited by the floods of these
times fainter imprints, vaguer contours than those
left by Henry Parkes. Why it will prove so
when the man’s work will itself lack permanence
will be one of the riddles of Fate. We who are
close to him, who knew him in the breathing and
aggressive flesh, have assuredly failed to under-
stand Parkes, or to explain him ; and lack-
ing that intimate personal knowledge of the
greatness manifestly his in some respects, and
of the meanness as indubitably his in other
respects, with which alone it is possible to obtain
something like an accurate understanding of his
complex nature, how shall the student in distant
ages see the man as he was and understand his
strength, his weakness, or that implication of the
one and the other which was really his dominant
characteristic ?
* * * *
An appreciation of such a personage must neces-
sarily be imperfect. It is a philosophic common-
place that the historian will understand the man
who has bulked hugely in the public eye better
than the man’s own contemporaries, because he
sees him in perspective ; but it may be questioned
whether Parkes, the exception to so many rules,
may not be an exception to this also. Wrote
Newman to Manning, “ I find it difficult to
understand you.” Manning, upon this, might
have made mental comment, “I find it difficult
to understand myself.” Parkes has said, to the
writer’s positive knowledge, in one of those
moments of frankness with which, intercalated
with unnecessary and mischievous reticences, he
would as often as not distinguish a conversation :
“I cannot understand myself.” Yet however
dilatory and remittent in other particulars
in the labor and pain of self-analysis, Parkes
never failed. It was his pride, to the last hour of
his intellectual life, to know himself as no others
of the thousand who endeavored to gauge his
personality and measure his limitations could
possibly know him. Not infrequently does it
happen that a person who takes up such an atti-
tude is absolutely transparent. His self-revela-
tions are the easier to read because they are
unconscious. With Parkes, however, the case is
far different. I he mental force, the self-control,
the faculty and power of dramatic action possessed
by him m the years of his public life, were
such that it is very doubtful whether any man
intimately since he first mounted
th ,e i plane ° f political distinction could
have truly said upon reflection that he had
never misrend’ never been deluded, by what
he had once regarded as a perfectly Uncon-
scious unfolding by Parkes of his temperament
h.s desires his ambitions. Parkes, great in some
* lttle m , so ? lany thin gF never showed
his littleness so clearly as in the rein art, ki
strength and variety of his capacity as an actor

The essence of dLE c ’ of Ffe.
the actor with the charaSacted’^ ° f
slaving the rdle of a statesman and a patriot
during what must be distinguished as the second
enoch of his life, was never whole-hearted and
whole-minded. He was a histrion and he knew
it His life can only be welded into consistency
with itself upon this theory.
* * * *
“ During the second epoch ’’—for there were twc
p-reat divisions in Parkes’s life. No perceptible
fine of demarcation separates them, because in so
psychologically complex a nature it is impossible
to put the finger upon the day or year when certain
admirable qualities ceased to influence the man s
growth or when the vitalising sap was transformed
into a corrupting poison Yet it is not impossible
to discern a time when there was a differentiation
and a change in the essentials of Parkes s charac-
ter—a differentiation which progressed to contrast,
and a change for the worst. That time lies within
a vear or two, on one side or the other, of the in-
auguration of Responsible Government in New
South Wales. Parkes, the fervent-spirited patriot,
with the virile passion and sweeping imagination
of a large-minded statesman, died—tortured to
death under the weights, heavy and coercive, of
debt and financial disaster. Parkes, the oppor-
tunist and the histrion ; Parkes, who looked upon
life as a fiction, humanity as a puppet, politics as a
farce, patriotism and public spirit and freedom as
terms with which to juggle away the senses of the
people as conjurors juggle away the vision of their
patrons; Parkes, the charlatan—was born. In
that pseudo-autobiography of his, “Fifty Years
in the Making of Australian History, he penned
a phrase which stirred hundreds to loud laughter.
During the time when he was conducting the
Empire he passed through such storms and stress
that to have endured and survived “he must,” he
wrote, “ have been of the stuff of which martyrs
are made.” Parkes a martyr ! A fine joke, truly !
Was he not a humorist rather in thus writing?
Nay. Parkes—a humorist and a farceur in the
composition of most chapters of that book— to
write that sentence, dipped his pen into the very
reservoir of his life-blood; for in the mighty
struggle to keep the Empire alive, to pay his way,
to feed his family, to fight public foes and private
antagonism, and to resolutely pursue his ideal to
make the paper the trusted organ of an educated
democracy, he mounted to heights of self-abnega-
tion which can be described with truth only in
the term Martyrdom.
* * * *
Egotist always, in the first period as in the
second, Parkes was, till he wrote his last lines for
the journal he had founded and nourished with his
being, filled with the superb egotism of the martyr-
heart, between which and selfishness there is little
distinction. Well would it have been for the
splendour of *his record, the lustre of his fame, the
value of his achievements, and the lasting good of
these young countries, if he had been brave enough
and wise enough to continue to live in the in-
spiring atmosphere of self-devotion to great prin-
ciples. But once occasion had passed, Parkes
faltered, craved for less ethereal air, lost faith in
the ideals and purposes of humanity, furled the
wings of his soul. Behind him, to the moment
the control of the Empire passed out of his hands,
he had a life-story of strenuous endeavor and
of a noble, if imperfect, self-culture. It had
been stained, no doubt, by blunders, but the
mighty strength of the man, which had conquered
privation and ignorance and contumely, to that
moment had been ever on the side of the masses,
of right, of political and intellectual freedom.
With the fall of the Empire, Parkes fell. His
feet had been treading the path to the goal of
greatness; from that path he diverged at an
almost imperceptible angle, feeling that he could
regain it when and where he chose. Never yet,
however, in the history of individual man did
the glossy purples of true greatness droop a
second time to a point within his grasp. That
angle of divergence, as the years passed, became
wider and wider. Parkes had no choice but to
proceed along the path of deviation, and it has
brought him, after forty years—years of a dazzling
popularity, alternating with an abasement no less
remarkable—to a tomb whence scarce a soul out
side the circle of his friends would recall him.
* * * *
In the battle against transportation and in the
strife that preceded the establishment of Respon-
sible Government, Parkes (let it be ever remem-
bered to his honor !) was a foremost combatant on
behalf of popular rights. The truth was plain to
him then which he lost sight of in the years of his
political eminence—that there is little merit in a
political system that has not for its ultimate end
the improvement of social conditions and the
emancipation of society from the slavery imposed
by the ignorance and the poverty of the masses.
The iron of suffering had entered into Parkes’s
inmost nature; the fiery oratory of the Chartist
Pemberton (to whom in'his heart of hearts Parkes
bore the reverence and gratitude which one man
owes to another who has awakened within him the
desire to know and the capacity to think, and
who has given him a mastery over methods of
utterance) had planted in his intellect the germs
of a Liberalism which never wholly ceased to
fructify, however events in after-life mocked the
Liberal spirit; and the possibilities of colonial
development aroused his imagination and fer-
tilised his understanding. Never an exact or
an analytic reader, and deprived of the invaluable
aid to development which stimulative teachers can
communicate, Parkes had yet read widely, and from
die arsenal of English and American political
literature he drew- many a weapon to wield in the
great fights of the time. Against the territorial
privilege which the imperial authorities and most
of the landed and commercial classes would have
built up upon a renewal of transportation, and
against the privilege of a titled caste, for which
entworth, traitor to his own past and blind to
i e trend of his own actions, was manceuvring,
rarkes fought ably, and with that reckless expen-
! 1 ure ,°1 health and strength which at all times
e used in conflict. He was a great tighter then,
and a successful one. He won political pro-
minence and success by newspaper articles and
P a orm speeches. Nevertheless, in his triumphs
he consummated his private ruin. Correspondence
as yet unpublished, when it appears in the light of
i iy, will reveal vvhat machinations, having for
ieir aim his financial entanglement, were resorted
v r y L Credlt ° rS wh £ were hi political alliance with
titf T- . ve ?® a ' r i 3 es -, 1 or two or three years he staved

could sh oT es^r with the comment: “I
at all evo , ? th T ’ He could write then—prose,
are thf>v ? i.Fht’noteworthy as these biographies
brtlliancv far short > point of force and
editorial 5 !, i° some of his contributions to the
Forster Deniehy, Lang, Martin,
delights f yn » shared with him the labors and
° ‘ comment in the Empire during the


contest ; and comparative study of the respec-
tive writings of that little body of publicists, each
of whom was a master of brilliant and incisive
newspaper polemics, demonstrates that Parkes,
deficient in all the finer elements of culture of
which the others could boast, was in the excel-
lence of his literary works no whit inferior to any
one of them. Yes, Parkes could write, and he
could edit, and were his friends wise, they would
inscribe on his gravestone the words, “ He edited
the Empire in 1853-G.” The statutes, a long list
of which in the epoch of his decadence he was
fond of reciting as evidence of his statesmanship,
are nothing as intrinsic work, or as contributions
to formal legislation, compared with the shaping
and welding impulses that Parkes and the men
whom he influenced imparted to the body politic
in those fateful years.
* * • •
It has been a source of amazement to those who
were familiar with the magnificent virulence of
his opposition to the introduction of a hereditary
system into Australia, that he should have weakly
displayed in his later course an almost toadyish
appreciation of titles, and of the patronage of
titled personages. But Parkes, after the sixties,
was not the Parkes of the early constitutional
struggle. He had become cynical as to the
value placed by the masses, upon efforts for
their improvement; the “ arch-anarchist,” as
Wentworth had dubbed him most unjustly,
had had his fangs drawn. His re-entrance
into politics was marked by treacheries to
both men and principles ; his egotism now be-
came excuseless ; henceforth he played his part.
The power he had once sought because power was
an instrument of reform ceased to be magnetic,
and while he hungered still, and never ceased to
hunger, for power, he sought it to gratify a vanity
soon to become insatiable, and a spirit of revenge
-which could rejoice in calling to heel the “ crea-
tures ” that had once despised him. Titles, the
company of titled personages, the glitter of
imperialistic trumpery, of all that was symbolised
by the laced coat and silken breeches of the Wind-
sor uniform, and the ribbon and star of the
Michael and George, gained an added importance
in his sight, but not because of the reason com-
monly ascribed to him. While his intellect
remained virile, he estimated these things at their
true worth—he knew them for badges of servitude
and the insignia of flunkeys. Nevertheless, as
power for vanity’s sake was now to him a thing
desirable, and as the classes whom he now
sought to use looked upon the tokens of
flunkeyism as something which it was glori-
ous to possess, Parkes marched with the
powdered and the beplushed hating their
servility of soul and certainly hating himself for
using them. His statesmanship had resolved
itself into the employment of individual mean-
nesses for what he considered as the public good.
This explains how
This hind, of princes peer,
preferred at last to ally himself to the men to
whose thought a peer’s smile was heaven, and a
“riband to stick in their coats” a dower of
dignity. Amid the wreckage of his fortunes and
the shadows of failure he wanted the courage to
wait till the fitting moment returned for the re-
assertion of his native liberalism; and his exalta-
tion, he reasoned, was soonest obtainable by
making stepping-stones of prostrate souls. That
was Parkes’s cardinal blunder ; it accounted for
much, if not everything, of the O’Farrell shame—
it explains his alliances with the ultra-Tories of
his later Governments. Knowing well what he
was doing, and well weighing the cost, he reso-
lutely
Broke from the van and the freemen,
Sank to the rear and the slaves.
* * * •
“ The burning brand of shame ” upon Parkes’s
political life is not, however, his failure to make
his career of power and distinction harmonious
with his early aspirations : it is his conduct with
respect to the O’Farrell episode. The volume of
good produced by his legislative and administra-
tive acts may perhaps be measured not inac-
curately ; but it is beyond human calculation to
estimate the evils generated by his infamous
speeches at Kiama and Jamberoo, and by his pro-
ceedings as Colonial-Secretary in the Martin
Ministry, following upon the attempted assassina-
tion of the Duke of Edinburgh. To plant, and
once planted, to carefully nurture, the seeds of
religious feuds in the soil of a nascent nation, the
political system of which was not irreligious
because it gave the freest scope to all religions:
this was a crime against the Australian race.
Those seeds Parkes planted, and he nursed the
tender plants till they grew to monster luxuri-
ance. No defender of his course at the O’Farrell
time can ever raise the plea in his behalf that
lie erred in ignorance, and from a short-sighted
indifference to the consequences of his policy.
The very persons who profited by his actions dare
not now pronounce a syllable in his defence ; and
that he knew what the verdict of posterity would
be upon his deeds and words is clearly to be seen
from the care he has taken to preserve his version.
Not in his “Fifty Years” will that version be
found, but among the archives of a State institu-
tion in an Australian province—not the province
he cursed by his conduct —is enrolled a document
which will reveal to the people of a generation yet
unborn what Henry Parkes thought of himself at
the hour when he sent O’Farrell to his doom.
That document and a letter to Thomas Carlyle
will form his exculpation, and—of exculpation
he will receive no title.
* * * *
Of the massive mental qualities of the man there
is no space to write adequately. In oratory, there
flashed from him at times passages of electric
significance and of rhetorical vividness ; but a
really illustrious orator he was not, because he
never delivered, during recent years at least,
“ a great speech ” in which there was no false note,
no transgression of taste, no lapse into jejuneness
or chicanery. As an administrator, he lacked
capacity to master detail, and to evolve order
from a chaos of particulars. Saturated with the
shibboleths of English constitutionalism, he yet
had no sense of the action and interaction of
forces through which historical tendencies make
themselves manifest. As a constructive states-
man, he had recourse always to models or to
lieutenants. As the leader of a party, he was at
his best in fair weather : in the face of well-
organised and aggressive opposition he could
never rise'to those master-strokes of policy which
disarm while they defeat. As the head of a
Cabinet he could not utilise the vast potentialities
of men of strong individuality ; he could only
lead weaklings, for whom he cared so little that
he would not disguise from them that he spurned
while he led. Without the higher characteristics
of generalship, wanting which no politician merits
the name of statesman, he could not form com-
binations on a large scale. Certainly, as a parlia-
mentary strategist, he has only been equalled in
N.S. Wales by Cowper, and as the creator ot a
supixirt in the electorates on behalf of measures
for the principles of which the people had no in-
stinctive regard, the mother-province has never
seen his like. Who but Parkes could have swept
in tornado fashion through the country in 1887 and
m 1889 : Who but Parkes, of all Australasian
politicians, could have taken a series of dull ab-
stractions and have breathed into them such
vitality as the Federal movement possessed in
1889 possesses indeed to-day ? Yet in everything
failure ever attached to him—perfected results to
nothing. In his popular leadership he led, at the
last, weird shadows : in Parliament, a coterie of
spineless traitors. His latest electoral appear-
ances are among the most pitiable incidents of
Australian political life. Their one demonstrable
interest lay in their revelation of the abyss into
which he had been precipitated, and of the shame-
lessness of the erstwhile followers who had plunged
him into the lower deeps of those lowest depths.
* » * *
The man’s actual heart was, strangely though it
may sound, not in politics but in literature, in
history, and in art. There was a singular vein of
sentiment in his nature which found no appro-
priate vent in his public existence, but which was
best understood through the conditions of his
home life. He slept for years in a room on which
the principal wall ornaments were the portraits of
the Commonwealth men—Cromwell, Pym, Hamp-
den. To see him handle a letter of Tennyson’s or
Carlyle’s, or the simple autographs of Lincoln,
was to receive a lesson in reverence. Books and
other mementoes of the illustrious dead were to
him the wine of life. And yet he was no scholar
—scarcely even to be termed a student. As to his
own place in Literature, his poems are a by-word ;
his later prose work already forgotten.
* * * *
Of the moral element in Parkes’s nature, there
is no need to speak here, save in regard to that one
element of magnanimity, the note of a truly great
man. Of this quality, what evidence is there in
his speeches or his policy? He could taunt an
adversary on the floor of Parliament, and in the
same breath deride that opponent for having made
him —Parkes —a gift in terms which contained a
reference to circumstances respecting which smaller
men would have been dumb. He could fling to a
starving poet a Government post, and remind him
in the instant that he attached his signature to
the letter of appointment that the poet had once
held him—Parkes —up to ridicule.
♦ * * *
Henry Parkes is dead. He has died in the
fullness of years, and one wishes one could say
with perfect truth in the fullness of honor also ;
but to have died with honor laurelling his memory
he should have died long since. He has sur-
passed in length of life most public men, as he has
surpassed most in opportunities. But smaller
men have done more, because they were truer
men. And if we may not say that the nation
mourns him as it mourned Higinbotham and
Ballance, we can say that it hears of his death
with a regret sincere and poignant. Not because
of what he has done, however, but because of
what he has left undone.
* * * *
And so,leaving him in the company of the august
departed, to those mute memorials which have
fallen upon his grave-from the hands of those in
whose veins runs his blood, from the hands of
friends who feared while they obeyed, from the
hands of foes who admired while they feared, let
there be added only this phrase : “ He once stood
within an ace of greatness.”
P.W.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Mixed Railway Gauges.
Premier Reid proposes a simple method of
getting over the break-of-gauge difficulty at
Albury. His suggestion is that Victoria should
alter the railway from Albury to Melbourne to
the N.S. Wales gauge, and thus make things
right—presumably at its own expense—so far
as the main line is concerned. Considering
that the whole difficulty was caused by N.S.
Wales, which decided upon the broad gauge at
the beginning, induced the Victorians to adopt
that costly system for the sake of uniformity,
and then abandoned it and left Victoria in the
lurch, G. H. Reid’s proposal that 1 - Victoria
should stand the racket is somewhat interesting.
In any case this isn’t the time for alterations.
The Federal railway management will some
day have to fix up a uniform gauge for all
Australia, and the decision should be left to it.
At present there are in this continent about 2500
miles of line at 4ft. (the N.S. W. gauge) ;
about 3600 miles on the Victorian sft. 3iu.
gauge; and about 3900 miles on the cheap
3ft. 6in. gauge adopted by Queensland and
Westralia, and latterly by S.A. These are the
State railways. Westralia has all its private
lines on the 3ft. 6in. basis. The private lines
of S.A. are partly 3ft. 6in. and partly sft. 3in.
Of the four private lines of N.S.W., one is
sft. 3in. gauge ; one is 3ft. 6in. ; and two are
4ft. Bgin. All which diversities seem expressly
calculated to prevent one province assisting
another with troops or anything else in case of
that emergency which we hear about so often,
and to make the proposed Federal army prac-
tically useless. If an enemy turned up at
Brisbane, say, the reinforcements from Victoria
would be stranded at Albury until N.S.W.
could get rolling-stock accumulated to carry
them through, and as the N.S.W. rolling-stock
would probably be busy rushing that province’s
own reinforcements to the Q. border, the
chances are that the Vic. soldiers would be
left stranded at Albury indefinitely. Then,
when they did get through, they and the
N.S.W. men would be wrecked at Wal-
langarra until Queensland could find carri-
ages and locomotives to bring them along the
rest of the way, and as a lot of its rolling-
stock would probably be in the hands of
the enemy, and the supply is rather scarce at
all times, the probabilities are the troops would
have to walk the 232 miles to Brisbane, and by
the time they got there the enemy would have
left and would be busy sacking Melbourne and
annexing all the preference shares in the recon-
structed banks. The break of gauge is a great
institution even now, but Australia will never
quite realise how great it is until some event
like this comes to pass, and its army is stranded
at wayside stations all over the continent, and
its commissariat is aground at a dozen separate
junctions, and its ammunition blows up while
being constantly shifted from one series of
trucks to another, and chaos sets in generally.
A Very Old Case.
The case of Mrs. Kilpatrick v. Hdddart,
Parker and Co. is still progressing gradually
in Melbourne. Plaintiff’s husband was drowned
in 1893 by the foundering of the more-or-less-
coffin-ship Alert. Mrs. Kilpatrick sued the
owners for damages on the ground that they
sent out their craft in an unseaworthy condi-
tion, and that her husband lost his life and she
and her child were deprived of their bread-
winner through gross neglect or greed or
callous indifference, or something of the sort.
She got a verdict for £6OO. Then the shipping
firm moved for a new trial, and it was allowed,
and after months of sickening delay.the new
trial came off, and again Mrs. Kilpatrick won,
the verdict this time being ,£791. Next, the
firm appealed to the Full Court, and the case
was argued a third time last February, and
judgment was reserved. Now' the Full Court
has announced that it really can’t make up
its mind, and has ordered that a still fuller
Court, consisting of all the Judges save the one
engaged in the original trial, shall hear the dis-
mal case all over again and consider it some
more. The Court has now been wrestling with
this case off and on for two years. It has accu-
mulated such a stupendous bill of costs that
even if it is finally decided that the widow
Kilpatrick has suffered a grievous wrong, and
she is ordered to receive her £791, she won’t
get a farthing—it will all go in paying her
share of the costs, and probably her furniture
and her other possessions will go as well. If
the case is decided against her, she will also be
ruined by costs —in fact, she will be ruined by
costs either way. The relief the law professes
to give her is a mockery. She has won two
cases ; she has got two verdicts ; and simply
because the defendants are rich, and are able to
prosecute one appeal after another, she hasn’t
got, and never will get, a farthing. If she
had lost her case at the first trial she
couldn’t have appealed for want of money
to pay costs, therefore the wealthy litigant
has four chances against the poor litigant’s
one —saying nothing of the fact that there is
still the Privy Council to appeal to, making a
fifth chance in favor of the men with money.
A good deal might be said about the ghastly
meanness of the shipping firm which uses its
wealth to swamp a widow and an orphan with
law-costs, as well as about the legal system
which allows it to do so. But the interesting
feature just now is the self-acknowledged
ignorance and incompetence of the highly-paid
judiciary, which is responsible for this latest
delay and this last outbreak of costs. The Vic.
bench has heard the story of the lost Alert
three times in all its exhaustive detail, and it
simply states now that it is so dense or so weak
and vacillating that it must hear it all over
again at vast expense before it can come to a
conclusion, and it doesn’t promise to come to a
conclusion even then. The court is paid by
the people to decide such matters on the
evidence ; if it can’t decide when it has heard
the evidence, then it should be sacked for
incompetence. If a £791 case has to be yelled
into the court’s ear four times, at a cost
of about £5OO a time, before the court can
hear it, then it doesn’t matter a straw how
mathematically accurate the final decision is; if a
just verdict for £791 costs £lOOO or £2oo* > to get,
then it would have been better to have got an
unjust one at a cost of £5O. The much-vaunted
purity of the Bench isn’t the thing it is cracked
up to be. A venal Bench which took a <£loo
bribe from the ricli litigant and definitely threw
out the poor litigant on her back hair at her
first appearance, and before the costs had time
to accumulate, would be a lot cheaper than the
present kind, and would give the poor litigant
quite as good a chance as he or she now has—
which is no chance at all.
Mildura.
Chaffey Bros. (Ltd.), the famous Mildura irri-
gation concern, has now reached the awful-
disclosure stage, and the reason for the collapse
becomes fully apparent. Chaffey Bros, got
their irrigation concession from the Victorian
Government. Then they floated it into a co.
and took £200,000 worth of paid up shares and
<£2500 cash for their “interest.” The public
could only be persuaded to take £44,770 worth
of shares, so, as the Chaffevs apparently put
no money into the co., this £44,770 was all the
capital at the back of the stupendous enter-
prise. Then money was raised by selling
£114,900 of 5 per cent, debentures, but as
<£23,395 went in flotation expenses, commission,
brokerage or boodle, tiieco. was really paying
£6 13s. 4d. per cent, for the cash it actually re-
ceived. In addition, £57,000 worth of deben-
tures were issued and pawned to satisfy credi-
tors. The co. sold its land to settlers on time-
payment and took their mortgages for the
balances owing. It pawned the mortgages in
various lots to five banks, one life assurance
society, and 13 private firms, for money
to go on with. Then it pawned its unsold
land to three banks and six private firms.
It gave the guarantee of a prominent director
for £50,000 to four banks. It pawned its
machinery. It also put its bonds “up the
spout ” with two banks, one insurance society,
and three private creditors ; and finally it gave
second mortgages and pawned its immortal
soul, till it bust up for good. The co., with its
great dead-w'eight of British finance on its head,
was practically born dead. Mildura started
simply on the child-like faith of the Australian
in the English capitalist—-who, as often as not,
hasn’t any capital. Having neither capital nor
credit behind it, it worked from the beginning
on money borrowed at rates which no enter-
prise could stand. The borrowed money mostly
went in paying the expenses incurred in bor-
rowing itself, and in paying interest on itself
during the year or two that had to elapse before
Mildura began to make any returns worth men-
tioning. Then it was necessary to float another
loan to pay interest on the first loan, and a third
loan to pay interest on the first two, and so on
till the thirty-second loan or thereabouts was
floated to pay interest on the other thirty-one or
thereabouts. Some of the loans cost, it would
seem, as much as 9 per cent. The co. purports
to owe or to have paid one director £16,731 for
guaranteeing payment of its debts, said guaran-
teed debts now amounting to £50,000. It also
purports to owe him £3472 for services rendered
and £16,131 for interest. Out of the alleged
paid-up capital of £474,770, only £44,770
appears to have ever been paid in cash. There
is no money left, and no assets that are not in
pawn, and no one knows “ where ’e are.” Like
the Maoriland Midland Bail way Co., the Mil-
dura concern has been a ghastly, unspeakable
muddle and a monument of incapacity from
beginning to end ; and of all the financial mud-
dles which Australia has produced in the last
ten years—the Davies companies, the Mercan-
tile, Federal and City of Melbourne banks, the
Commercial of S.A., the Premier Building
Society, and the busted land co’s all included —
there are none that have equalled this pair of
enterprises in their monumental failure. If the
Australian can keep on believing in the sagacity
of the foreign capitalist after these two exam-
ples, his faith must be very enduring indeed.
The Brisbane Eight-Hours’ Demonstration com-
mittee should be content with a very mild
“crow” over the Lamington cheque episode.
They simply laid themselves down in the young
man’s way, and, mistaking them for political
reptiles, he superciliously walked over them.
Why did the committee start the unsavory
business by tapping the Governor for a cheque,
in the first instance ? He was an utter stranger
to Queensland and Australian parties generally.
As soon as he or his like lands on Australian
soil, every creeping organisation endeavors to
capture him as patron, bottle-holder, or cash-
dispenser, and it is only natural that in some
cases he should fail to distinguish the good
from the bad among such a swarm of
prostrate beggars. Gov. Lamington, in effect,
put his case" this way, and the incident,
instead of inspiring the committee to play the
role of indignant Roman fathers, should im-
press upon them the propriety, if in similar
circumstances they make up their minds to
beg again, of first calling upon the new poten-
tate and explaining to him in well-turned
language that they are crawlers of a wholly
innocuous type, politically and socially, and
that, so far from meaning disrespect to Consti-
tution or Throne, they will give him guarantees
that his cheque shall be spent in soft drinks,
colored banners, and crying “Hurrah !”
Melbourne Reform Club, an institution of fat
men and political economists of an obsolete but
malignant type, will be careful before it again
invites Rev. Reginald Stephen to lecture to it
on “Freedom of Contract.” The members sat
in puzzled horror the other night while he told
them that absolute freedom of contract was
neither desirable nor admirable, and that every
civilised state had recognised the fact. Free-
dom, they were informed, was not the end, but
only a means to an end, and wherever it did
not conduce to the general good, it was wise to
limit it. He claimed that society had the right
to fix limitations to freedom of contract in in-
dustrial relations :
(1) As to the conditions under which the work should
be done, as with the Factories Act. [Fancy the wincing of
the Argus as it recorded this. ]
(2) As to the rate of wages to be paid.
(3) As to the qualification of the laborer for the work.
Of course, by this time Fatmanity was getting
ropeable, and the rev. lecturer pityingly threw
a little sop in the shape of a mild disapproval
of the Queensland shearers’ strike.
The Coffin-Ship.
Do you think that the crew would go to sea without
making any complaint? —Yes, they would have to go for
the sake of their wives and children ; in order to get
bread, men have to put up with a good deal nowadays.—
Evidence , re the unseaworthiness of ships generally,
given before a board of inquiry in Melbourne the other
day.
She may be an iron cullender, an’ cranky as a
rack,
With rotten decks an’ rotten plates, or rotten
in the bulk,
But we’ve got to take her out, my lads ; we’ll
maybe bring her back
If our luck is not ez rotten ez this God-for-
saken hulk.
For the kiddies must be fed,
An’ the missus have a bed,
An’ have clothes to hide her bones, my lads—a
roof to hide her head.
We who man the good old coffin-ship have need
of marrer bones,
An’ good cause to bear in mind the bloomin’
mission covies’ tex ;
There’s a flake ’r two o’ rust between our souls
an’ Davy Jones,
An’ a tidy swag o’ cargo gaily swung around
our necks.
When we’re bobbin’ on the seas,
We pray God to hold His breeze,
An’ our teeth begin to rattle when we hear a
squeaker sneeze.
When we swing her out, an’hearken to the
lau’sman’s kindly wishes,
Ho ! it’s so-long to the missus, an’ it’s good-
by, mar an’ par,
Ez we’re proberly a-steamin’ out to picnic with
the fishes
On a pile o’ perished iron, when it ain’t a blob
o’ tar.
If she lives to agonize
’Tween the water an’ the skies
’Till we make a port, we’ll take it ez another
glad surprise.
Sun and moon shine through her scurvy decks,
her venterlation’s fair,
For she gapes in fifty places when she rises
to the swell.
When the sea gets up an’ hits her, then we wait
to feel her tear,
An’ we wish the crimson owners was a-board
to hear her yell.
Yes, we wish them swells was aft
On their crazy, leakin’ craft,
With a ragin’ sea before her, an’ a rousin’ squall
abaft.
All her boilers spit an’ hiss an’ spirt—they’re
cankered up with rust
But we’ve got to make a livin’, an’ that ain’t
concernin’ us.
We go downward if she breaks, my lads, or
up’ards if they bust —
The direction doesn’t matter, an’ we mustn’t
make a fuss.
For the kiddies must be fed,
An’ the missus have a bed,
An’ have clothes to hide her bones, my lads —a
roof to hide her head.
EDWARD DYSON.

The promised ensuement of “Thorough-
bred ’ ’ at Sydney Cri. proved to be, on the whole,
an unsatisfying circumstance. Lumley’s farce has
no recognised centre. It is suggestive of a long
word with the accent nowhere, or a story where
everybody or nobody is the hero, or the two ends
of anything with the middle left out. There
is a lot of amusing incident in it, but there is no
central incident in particular. Either the play is
mainly about the trouble Brough gets into as a
pious man with a surreptitious racehorse, or the
difficulty Boucicault has in making love coherently
to Miss Katherine Hardy, or Miss Ea.ber’s reluct-
ance to marry Titheradge because he is an Ameri-
can, or Dorrington’s fatuous aspect as a poet, or
something else. “ Thoroughbred ” contains a good
many amusing points, but the points are mostly
as disconnected as an early Christian in the Coli-
seum. The dialogue is smart, and, as almost all
the characters are the leading character and every
character is running on his own detached hook,
the co. shows out very well. Titheradge’s
Amurrikan is a fine piece of subdued humor,
and when he mixes the great national cocktail
and gives up trying to be English, the soul of the
audience goes out to him unanimously. Bouci-
cault’s festive youth and Brough’s henpecked mil-
lionaire are both excellent characters, and Dor-
rington, as a slow, long-haired bard, blooms into
unexpected brilliance. Miss Faber produces good
results out of no materials in particular, except a
pair of bright eyes and a general permission from
the author to hang round, and Miss Hardy does
likewise. A promiscuous song-and-dance enter-
tainment shows the co. in an altogether new
light, and suggests that Boucicault and Miss
Temple are a woeful loss to the variety-stage ; and
when Miss Watt-Tanner grovels bulkily at the
feet of her outraged husband, she presents the
broken-hearted girl of melodrama in a novel and
improved, as well as enlarged, aspect. All that
there is in “Thoroughbred,” however, is due to
the company. There isn’t enough comedy to hold
the people together, and, with a worse company,
it would begin to be doubtful if there was any
comedy at all.
*# * *
The great police horror is having a night
out at Her Majesty’s. “In the Queen’s Name ”
is a furious rush and stampede of policemen from
beginning to end. It opens with eight policemen
standing in a row, and it closes with Miss Lilia
Wilde fiercely shaking a dead policeman and pour-
ing shrill vituperations into the ear which will
never again be opened to receive information. In
between these two episodes the feet of many
policemen pound incessantly on the rotten stair,
and the voice of the innumerable cop demands
that the decaying door shall be opened in the
name of the law ; and the red visage of the flabber-
gasted bobby looks out of every window and sees
the assassin escaping over the house-tops ; and the
sad, sympathetic blue-bottle won’t let the weeping
wife come in and see her husband hanged ; and the
stolid, stony trap does sentry-go on the gallows,
and the peeler is on the full jump everywhere.
“In the Queen’s Name ” isn’t a new drama ;
it is only the old “ Secrets of the Police,”
or something to that effect, disguised and
with variations. Miss Lilia Wilde is practically
the whole show —or, rather, all that part of it which
isn’t merely the rush of infuriated constable. She is
partly larrikiness, partly handsome she-fiend, partly
affectionate fish-fag, and partly gutta-percha
costermonger, mixed with Topsy and various
other things. She does more rushing and jump-
ing than all the rest of the co. put together ; she
keeps up her end of a rousing fight with the vigor
of a bushel of wild cats arid floors the enemy
twice ; she knocks sundry policemen out of shape
at an emergency—in short, Miss Wilde holds the
drama together and whoops it along all the time,
and yet manages to make the wild-cat heroine a
rather picturesque and loveable character when
she isn’t blackening the eye of the force or shaking
the defunct constable. Alfred Woods is principally
occupied in being hanged. Robert Inman devotes
himself mainly to the crawlsome variety of wicked-
ness. Johnson Weir has good points as the father
policeman who is “shook” after death ; and Harry
Leston is an all-pervading individual who does
fearsome gymnastics in escaping from the police.
As a whole, the drama may be summed up as a
large, raw, red horror which goes at a gallop all
through, and gives more incident and gallows
and hurried policeman to the sq uare inch than is
usual. Miss Wilde’s fight with the villain is especi-
ally recommended as an example to any lady who
meets a burglar in the night, and every lady should
see it that she may know how to tackle the burglar
in a large and vigorous spirit. The fact that
“Uncle Tom’s Cabin,” with a new minstrel
element in it, ensues next Saturday is a circum-
stance that is too sad to be dwelt upon.
** * *
“ Saved From the Sea,” staged at Sydney
Royal on Saturday, is a melodrama which makes
The Bulletin feel sad right through. There are
many hunky bad men in it, and the property goes
to the nearest villain as it always does when the
rich man dies, and everything is exactly as it is in
every other melodrama. Baker, as the hero, is
saved from a rather badly-managed sea of raging
green calico, under complicated circumstances,
but this fact has no particular connection with the
story. Afterwards the law proceeds to hang
Baker on deficient evidence, but the trap won’t
work, and he is reprieved and sent to the old
stone-breaking yard at Portland to chip at the
same big block of stone which Rignold and Cates
and Augustus Glover and dozens of other stage
personages have tackled and given up as a bad
job. The stone-breaking scene has become a
misery and a weariness, and besides everybody
knows that the convict will presently escape
on the prompt side —the convict always escapes
on the prompt side. And it is equally a
matter of course that he will find the man who
can explain everything chipping at the other big
block of stone, because that person is always peg-
ging away mechanically at the other block. A
broken bridge, and a dead man sitting up in an
easy chair, and an explosion, and one or two other
trifles help to eke out the narrative, which doesn’t
end but only falls down dead in the presence of a
hay-waggon at the conclusion of Act IV. It
leaves the hero being hauled from his weeping
wife back to the stone-breaking job, but it is
vaguely assumed that he will be set free by-and-
bye because one of the conspirators has stated his
innocence to a policeman and then collapsed from
old age and gunshot before he had time to back up
his story with any details, or to attest it on oath,
or have it put in writing, or to explain coherently
why the other man really did the crime. It is
also assumed that the hero’s wife will get her
fortune from the other conspirator if he hasn’t
spent it all, because the same convict states to the
policeman, at the back of the hay-waggon and in
the presence of Bland Holt’s ruined hat, which
Mr. Bland has just jumped upon, that it
is really hers, but doesn’t even explain
which fortune it is. In short, the story has
all the beautiful consecutiveness of the romance
which the small boy laboriously composes in his
exercise-book and writes in a large hand with his
tongue out. Its best points are Baker’s fine act-
ing as the improbable hero, and the good work
done by Charles Brown and Miss Flora Anstead,
and the whole-souled manner in which Mrs. Bland
bounds on Bland’s hat—no other lady that The
Bulletin remembers ever bounded on a hat with
such airy abandon before or bounded so high.
Also, Miss Watson developes a lot of tragic inten-
sity, and promises rather better than she did, and
a very, very small boy named Smith does a piece
of child-acting which is far above the average.
* * * -*
Miss Myra Kemble’s benefit, dimly fore-
shadowed last week, takes place to-day (Thursday)
at the Lyceum. The “Firm’s” co. contributes
the most gorgeous act of ‘ ‘ Djin-Djin ” ; the B. and
B. co. gives one act of ‘ ‘ Thoroughbred ” ; and the
go’s from Her Majesty’s, the Royal, and the
Tivoli give an act each, after the scrappy fashion
of benefits in general. Miss Kemble, at one time
the favorite par excellence of Australian playgoers,
is now ill with a complication of complaints,
including partial paralysis, and her recovery is
practically hopeless. She has necessarily left for
good the stage of which she used to be such an
ornament, and in various other ways fortune has
deserted her. Therefore, the great heart of the
Australian people, which sent so many flowers to
Parkes after he was dead, is hereby invited to do
something large and really handsome for Myra
Kemble while she is yet alive and able to appre-
ciate it. The habit of waiting till an old friend
has gone and then sending a flower after him is
shiftless and unpractical, anyhow.

Fitzgeralds’ Circus opens at Sydney in all
its glory this (Thursday) evening, and it is gener-
ally reported that Solomon the Magnificent would
have dropped down dead on his ivory throne if he
had seen it. The Bulletin hopes to expand into
much loose eloquence on the subject next week.
* * * *
W. and M., in all probability, pass fewer
‘ ‘ dead-heads ” into their shows than any other
theatrical management on earth. The late Henry
Sampson, of London Referee, said something to
this effect after visiting M elb. Royal, during the
“ Firm’s ” regime, when he learned to his astonish-
ment that nearly everybody in the house had paid
for admission. The dead head record for Sarah
Bernhardt’s first appearance at the Princess’s must
have been unique. Many pressmen, “on the
door,” but not on the job as notice-writers, stayed
away from the great event under an impression
that they wouldn’t be admitted, and when the
curtain rose there were not more than half-a-dozen
stiff ’uns standing at the back of the dress-circle.
The writer, who was one of them, blushes at the
recollection.

The Bulletin has some feeling of respect
for “ Sons of Erin,” on account of its reckless
character, clever scenery, and hearty marmer
introducing itself to notice. Theactig
to only one objection, which couldn * avoide
by the players. They all do something that theji
did a fortnight earlier m The Enemy sCamp,
although their conduct on this occasion shows
more regard for common sense. Hans P ’
Frank Harcourt, and C. R. Stanford make them-
selves useful to the people already mentioned, and
Westmacott comes in late as a Yankee telegraph
operator, with cheerful results. As regards the
thunderbolt that whizzes across the chasm, it is
manifestly guided by the hand of Providence in
answer to the hero’s prayer. Therefore, N.S.W.
can depend upon Scot Inglis to burst up the n 3
drought with a few well-chosen words. As a
petitioner at the Throne of Grace he is much more
reliable than the Church, and his supplication is
far less snufflesome.
** * *
The Alexandra offers a revival of “First
Class,” wherein Dan Barry tames the spirit of his
ragged daughter by locking her in a dark cel ar,
to be chased by the rats. Dan is a partially-
clothed miser in the burglary trade, and the ettect
of the limelight gleaming down upon his eyeballs
and bare chest is impressive. He gets no apparent
benefit by his crimes, except the right to revel m
dirt and seclusion from the police, nevertheless he
goes on finding money for the gang, and the gang
flourishes for three hours at his expense. It he
didn’t drag his daughter round the premises by
her hair, Daniel might pass for a philanthropist.
Collett Dobson is the Roman-faced schemer who
always travels first-class until the police intervene,
and Miss Duggan shrieks earnestly among the
rats. The rest of this favorite Haleckian drama
is chiefly performed by Augustus Glover and a
confiding mother, who entrusts a friend by the
name of Nosey with half-a-sovereign, and waits
patiently for the change, which never returns.
** * *
“Trilby ” is now within a week of its
finish at Melb. Princess’s, but ere Svengali and
his song-bird depart for Sydney, The Bulletin
may as well mention a point in the first act which
would admit of more emphasis. It is Trilby’s re-
mark that Litolf, the composer, was struck by her
wonderful voice. Svengali expresses the impor-
tance of this statement by rising from the piano
and stopping the girl’s chatter, but the audience
doesn’t fully grip the situation, inasmuch as
Trilby’s words have almost escaped notice. If she
spoke them more incisively, rang them out with
some energy in the middle of a dead silence, the
house would understand that Svengali had
suddenly conceived a great scheme. Here,
as in other places, the performance seems to take
a universal acquaintance with the novel for
granted, instead of remembering that not 15 per
cent, of the audience is likely to know where the
germ of the tragedy is concealed. When Trilby
quotes Litolf she settles her fate. She gives the
Hebrew his hypnotic cue. She certainly ought to
give it for all it is worth.

Will Crackles, the latest London addition
to the Rickards Co., makes his first appear-
ance at Melb. Opera House to-night. Ken-
nedy (who happily got delayed awhile upon
the ocean) is appearing at the Athenaeum
for a limited season, which can’t be too
much curtailed, and Rev. Charles Clarke, an
entertainer of a very different color, will start his
limited season at the Town Hall next Saturday.
Nothing could be too nice to say of Charles Clarke’s
lectures, but the sweet things have been so often
said in this journal, where parsons, as a rule, are
not unduly worshipped, that a repetition of pre-
vious compliments must be taken as read in the
meantime. On the same evening the Newbury-
Spada Concert Co. will give tongue at the Exhibi-
tion, so Melbourne seems in a fair way to get more
extra shows than the city can carry.

“Cheer, Boys, Cheer,” the huge spec-
tacular drama with which George Rignold expects

country three months of steady preparation for
the shock.
At Poverty Point .
Madame Cooke, a sister of the famous Madame Celeste
and herself a one-time leading actress and manageress in
Australia, is a resident of Sydney. She is the oldest-
record actress on the continent. Over 80 years of ace
she yet has no need of Spectacles, and writes as firm a
hand as an ordinary commercial man. ’Twas Madame
Cooke who brought Lola Montez to Australia. She is
best known by her “Comic History of Australia,” pub
lished many years ago. i u

Re Australian playwrights : A Sydney manager, “ one
of the best,” says he has read, within the last couple of
years 55 “ new and original” dramas by local authors,
not one of which has been even possible of Production,
and the majority bordered on the ludicrous One (“ pat-
hetic”) play submitted was in 20 scenes, each scene being
laid in a different country. This was wntten by a well-
known alleged literary man, yet a crucial point of the
drama was the appearance of a lion and a kangaroo on the
banks of a’ river in France !
Unprejudiced testimony to Williamson’s powers as a
comedian came from some pressmen at a Melb. Royal
matinee some years ago. It was a show in aid of a
charity -the Dramatic Fund, perhaps and all the avail-
able big people were gathered in a performance of Trial
bv Jury ” The pressmen, who came late and couldn t
find a programme, recognised every notable member of
the crowd with the one exception of the judge, and his
Honor, they reckoned, was getting more humor (unob-
trusively) out of his character than any of them. The
judge turned out to be J. C. Williamson. He had been
casually criticised as an unknown actor, and classed in
the A 1 division.
Reuben Fax gets no “ butter” from minor members of
the “ Trilby ” Co. when verdant interviewers seek their
opinions as to the best artiste in the crowd. One thinks
Gecko is unsurpassable, another is lost in admiration of
Zou Zou. But the public vote for Fax, because his per-
formance is something they can swear by. Being re-
quired to hold the audience, he does it.
The much-execrated Little Billee at Melb. Princess s
serves one useful purpose, since he reconciles the people
to the death of Trilby in the fourth act. It is felt that the
end of the handsome heroine is very sad, but of two evils
death is far and away the smaller. Death is happiness
itself compared to the calamity of long life with Little
Billee-goat.
Dan Barry has introduced a family of trick bicyclists
into “ Uncie Tom’s Cabin.” “ Why don’t you put the
angels on bicycles in the apotheosis of Little Eva ? ” some-
body asked Daniel. “ Not a bad idea at answered
the manager, soberly. “By Jove ! I’ll see if we can’t
work it.” Manager Barry is the sort of man who, if called
upon to represent the golden streets of Elysium, would
want to drag in a steam-roller.
Connoisseurs declare La Belle Maie to be the prettiest
and shapeliest little woman seen on the Melbourne music-
hall stage within the memory of the baldest head. She
has ’em all on a wire.
Edith Crane, as Trilby, reminds the Melb. people of
somebody they remember well, and yet they can’t think
of the other woman’s name. Sarah Bernhardt it is. Crane
has cultivated a Sarah-like appearance about the eyes and
hair, very noticeable in some of her photos.
Miss Sylvia Madden, daughter of the Vic. Chief Justice,
has about the best nerve of any unprofessional warbler in
Melbourne. The attractive damsel, who sings intelli-
gently, makes her rather hard soprano go for twice as
much as the ordinary amateur organ of its class is worth
on a public platform. The same might be said of her
papa’s oratory. “ What is face-value ? ” asked a truculent
cross-examiner in court, the other day. “ Cheek,” replied
his learned friend.
“ Cheer, Boys, Cheer,” one of the plays which George
ftignold brings back to Australia after his three months’
English trip, deals chiefly with the Jameson raid.
The refusal of the Engiish Examiner of Plays to
license “Joseph of Canaan” G.R. thinks hardly consis-
tent ; for “ The Sign of the Cross,” which flings sacred
texts about vigorously, is going well in London, and is
attracting highly-devout audiences, who pray into their
hats during the intermission instead of going out to post
important letters.
On the departure, a few weeks ago, of a Sydney Wes-
leyan minister from his circuit, the members of a literary
society in connection with the church gave a farewell
picnic to his sons. The affair was very slow, so, by way
of compensation, a number of the hosts and hostesses
wound up the day by a visit to Harry Rickards’ Tivoli !

Manager Westmacott, of Melb. T.R., may retire from
management after his Melb. season and join forces with
George Rignold. At present he has most of George’s co.,
and the latter would be in a hole for mummers if West-
macott kept on.
Rignold brings with him from London a good many
plays, including “ Cheer, Boys, Cheer,” with which he
will in all probability open in Melbourne in August; and
“ Tommy Atkins,” which, although a frost at the West
End, is going great guns in the East. There is plenty of
good stuff in “ Tommy Atkins,*” which no doubt .the great
Henry V. will bring out. It is a rattling, rollicking sort
of play, and with the Jingo spirit rampant in the city of
snivelling and imposture it is bound to go down well.
Rignold has also up his sleeve a new play by Arthur Shir-
ley, one or two comedies, and a few other things.
The best photo, of the much-photographed
Parkes was the last, or very nearly the last, which
the veteran had taken. It was reproduced in last
week’s Bulletin, and was by Falk, of Sydney.
The excellence of the reproduction was almost
due to the artistic finish of Falk’s original,
which depicted an idealised Parkes—the Parkes
that was with the Parkes that might have been
looking out from his faded eyes and vaguely assert-
mg himself in the background.
