The Wild-Cat Column .
The A.M.P. Society’s report for 1895 is, on the
whole, a satisfactory document, though the effects of the
depression are manifest in many places. The renewal
premiums (£1,178,144) are £12,000 more than last year
and £20,000 more than in ’93. New premiums (£99 075)
are £BO.lO more than in ’94, and £2OOO more than in ’93
Also they are £26,000 less than in 1890. Interest on
investments ( 694,903) is £4OOO less than last year, despite
the fact that the funds have increased by £727,000 and
only £36,000 more than in ’93, when the funds were
£1,200,000 smaller. Total income (£2,009,843), however
shows an improvement of £39,090 over 1894, ami it is
noteworthy tnat ’95 is the first occasion w'hen the vear’s
receipts have got over the two-million level With a
much larger business the death and endowment claims
(£623,091) are only £167 more than in 18.14 ; exp uses
(8.37 per cent.) are practically the same ; and the funds
now amount to the magnificent total of £13,162,612.
The balance-sheet is, in most respects; a model of com-
pleteness. It sets forth separately interest in arrears,
interest due but within the days of grace (both of which
items are very moderate), interest accrued but net due,
properties acquired by foreclosure, office properties in
each province, premiums due and premiums overdue.
One feature is that properties foreclosed now represent
£538,454, nearly £200,000 more than last year, and
£350,000 more than two years ago. This looks as if a
good many of the society’s borrowers had lately been
getting into difficulties. It is a pity that rent is not re-
turned separately, as it was in 1890, so that the public
could judge if these foreclosed properties are good value
for the £538,434 set against them.
Surrenders this year amount to no less than
£375,283—£4500 more than in ’94, and £72,000 more than
in ’93. Interest has fallen from £5 13s. 6d. per cent, in
’93, and £5 11s. lOd. in ’94, to £5 ss. 2d. In consequence
of this and other causes, the surplus (£476,465) is fully
£200,000 less than that of ’94. The bonus will be about the
same, but this result is arrived at by adding only £16,000
to the reserve for depreciation, whereas the amount added
last year was £225,000. On the other hand, the A.M. I>. has
now reduced its assumed future rate of interest to £3 12s.
6d. per cent, in place of the former 4 per cent., and, when
it gets it down to 3) per cent, a year hence, a great piece of
work will have been completed, and thereafter bonuses
should improve. Taking it all round, the present result
is quite as satisfactory as could be expected.
* * * *
The report of the National Bank of Australasia
for the half-year ended 31st March, ’96 (which appears on
the inside of front cover in this issue) is another down-
ward-looking document. The net profit is £10,523, w'h ; ch
compares badly with the £13,762 earned for six months
ended Sept., 1895 ; £15,125 for half ended March, ’95 ;
£25,231 for half ended Sept.,’94 ; and £31,312 at March,
’94. The National stands on a very different basis from
some of its rivals, seeing that it has regularly paid 5 per
cent, dividends to its preference shareholders and has
honorably met the 4£ per cent, interest on its recon-
structed deposits and still shown a balance to the good ;
nevertheless, the fact remains that its earnings have
growm less and less every half-year since the reconstruc-
tion. The usual 5 per cent. div. on preference shares
absorbs £7644, leaving only £2879 to carry to profit
and loss, making the whole amount accumulated in
three years £12,436. As it would take £39,000 to
pay a half - year’s div. at 5 per cent, on ordinary
capital, and the National is £36,000 short of that
sum, the prospects of the ordinary shareholders look
pretty bleak. Moreover, the last of the reserve-fund
(£100,000) has now been written off to cover losses ; and
with no reserve, a fast-declining revenue, and a n et profit of
less than Us. 4d. per cent, on capital, the National’s con-
duct in fooling away £7644 in a div. to preference share-
holders looks wholly unwarranted. If the bank has made
£10,523 on one hand and has lost £lOO,OOO on the other,
there is no legitimate profit out of which to pay
dividends.
The National is now paying off about =£1. 000, 000
of its old 4*4 per cent, deposits, and the saving in interest
—if not swallowed up by another downward movement in
the profits—should make things look very much better next
half-year. The new deposits have increased during the
six months by nearly half-a-million, the cash on hand and
note circulation have improved, and everything shows an
improvement—except the profits. Considering that the
cash on hand is close on £2,000,000 and the liquid securities
are £1,140,000 more, the National could afford to take the
bull by the horns and release the remainder of its old
deposits in one act. It could get quite as many of them
back as it has any use for at 3 or 3j per cent., and it would
thereby save at the very least £50,000 a year in interest,
which it badly wants just now. After that, it should adopt
the wise policy followed by the Royal of Queensland, and
make it a hard-and-fast law that one-fourth of its profits
shall go to a reserve, and that the reserve shall be
separately invested in Govt, securities, and then the
National may expect to be a great and prosperous institu-
tion again—some day.
The old depositors, at all events, have done re-
markably well out of the National. Part of their money
was converted into preference shares, which have received
5 per cent, regularly, and the stipulated 4)4 per cent, has
been paid on the balance, so that they are really getting
£4 Us. per cent, all round. The preference share idea
was good enough for the Commercial and Colonial, but
for the National it was a woefully bad spec.
» * * *
According to the liquidators’ report, the col-
lapsed Mildura Co. owes the Colonial Mutual Life Assur-
ance Society £21,700, money lent on mortgage, and
£10,750 accumulated interest. The society has about
£1,800,000 invested, and its latest balance-sheet sets
forth that “rents and interest outstanding and accrued”
are just £17,705. The amount accrued but not due must
amount to something on so large a mass of securities,
and the rents due and accrued must also amount to some-
thing. Yet if £10,750 interest is owing on one loan,
which represents less than one-thirtieth of the whole
amount advanced on mortgage, it leaves only £7OOO to
Cover everything else. - Is that £17,705 the whole
amount of the rents and interest owing? If it
isn’t, why are the figures presented so as to look
as if it was, and how much more is owing? If it is, how
does the institution contrive to collect its money so
vigorously from all its other creditors that £7OOO covers
all other interest and rents due and accrued ? And is the
Colonial Mutual—and a good many other societies as well
—prepared to make that line read in future balance-
sheets “ All interest and rents due, &e. ” ? In the present
case the loan appears tobe based on reasonably good security
despite the fact that the Mildura Co. seems to be more or
less a personage who is also a director of the Colonial
Mutual, so that gentleman looks as if he had been to some
extent lending the society’s money to himself. But the
real trouble is that the £10,750 said to be due for interest
on one small advance doesn’t look as if it fitted in with
the £17,705 alleged in the balance-sheet to be the whole
amount outstanding on all the advances. If it does fit in,
then the fit is truly wonde-ful.

What is the value of a balance-sheet and an
«rt.floate? Quite recently the Carlton and
west End Breweries Co. (Melb.) issued a balance-sheet set-
ring torth that its properties were worth £672,000; that its
net assets were £1,143,000 and its liabilities £843,000;
leaving a clear surplus of £300,000. Then the Brewery
'"'O' proceeded to liquidate, which seemed quite unac-
countable for such a prosperous concern, and when a
snareholder took legal proceedings to stop these doings
the management filed affidavits to the effect that pro-
P er S> s et down in the balance-sheet at £672,000, was only
worth £312,000; that of £400,000 set down as debts due
7? tbeco. ) £340,000 was believed to be bad ; that out of
£480,000 uncalled capital V 400,000 was irrecoverable ; and
that instead of £300,000 surplus there was £400,000
d TL len f’' Said barrister Topp, the co’s representative :
Ihe plaintiff’s paid-up shares are worth nothing. The

ably instructed Topp to say this, and as a certain bank
offers to take 6s. Bd. in the £ in settlement of the co’s

ghastly discrepancy between this statement and the

“ Attorney-General Isaacs; “According to the

sheet was a mass of lies from beginning to end.” What
is the exact value of a balance-sheet and an auditor’s
certificate in Australia?
* * * * .
The Vic. butter export-trade is a whole essay on
the natural cussedness of people in general. The mail
companies formerly had a monopoly of the export busi-
ness, and charged high freights which seriously damaged
the trade. When their contract expired they refused to
make any serious reduction on the ground that the old
rates were only just payable, and another co. got the
Government contract. Thereupon the mail co.’s cut their
rates to the new figure, spent large sums on storage
accommodation so as to run against the Governmentstores,
arranged for facilitating advances on butter consigned
through them, and are scrambling madly for a fraction of
the business at a price which they previously declared
wouldn’t pay for the whole of it. The sharks and middle-
men and the Argus are with them, while the Government
and most of the producers back the new contractors, and
it is a very pretty row as things stand.

If the stock exchange barometer means any-

The Colonial Sugar Refining Co. has made
£85,166 profit for the half-year, and pays the usual div. at
10 per cent, per annum, absorbing £85,059. The most
remarkable feature about this vast concern is the
mechanical regularity with which it turns out almost
exactly the same amount of profit every six months.

The Eagle Tobacco Co. (Sydney) reports a loss
for the half-year of £754. No dividend has been paid
since October, ’94, and then it was a humble 3V> per cent.
Prospects generally gloomy ; shares (13s. paid) in weak
demand at 4s. ; no reserve ; shortage on profit and loss
account, £341.

At Cobar : “ The Mt. Drysdale mine has fallen
off considerably, and shareho'ders are somewhat anxious
as to the cause.” Possibly it is want of gold. They have

* * * *
The Mt. Aylin prospectus is out. The mine is
in the Nana Creek district (N.S.W.), of which a good
opinion has more than once been here expressed. Capital,
£60,000, in that number of shares; consideration, £6OO,
and half the shares fully paid. J. S. A. Taylor, C.E., M.E.,
says there are upwards of 130,000 tons of crushing stone
in sight, but does not make it clear how he arrives at such
an exposed tonnage. The sizes of five reefs are carefully
set out, but nothing is said re the length of the ore chutes
—a far more important consideration.
The upward movement in British B.H. shares,
though partly due to an English inquiry, arises in great
measure from the spread of opinion that in the fiat
ground on Block 16—the northern block —a big “make” of
sulphides will be found at depth corresponding with the
big “ make ” of oxidised ores on the flat ground of Block
11 (Proprietary Co.). The unexpected striking of the
Junction western chimney of ore has markedly increased
confidence in the northern end of the lode ; and as good
ore has been found both north and south of the British,
there are at least fair reasons for expecting good things
from the depressed ground in that c’aim, although the
outcrop, when it continues after this break, reappears
more to the west on the Junction.
* * * *
Block 10 concentrating plant is doing only fair
work ; it has been found necessary to make an extra-
ordinary number of small alterations and adjustments,
yet there is no reason to expect that the plant will not be
able to profitably treat the ore.
B.H. North, having begun to turn out sulphide
concentrates, is having constructed a tram-line to connect
with the Silverton and South Australian line to the sea-
board. Underground, this mine looks only tolerably
well; no big output of payable ore could be maintained
upon present developments.
Block 14 mill put up a record in the third week
in April : it put through 2721 tons (including purchased
ores), getting 865 tons concentrates “of the usual fair
value ’’-—that is all the information ever conveyed on this
point. Besides that, 219 tons carbonate ore were sent
away.
J * * » *
The latest Broken Hill venture on the market is
the Junction South, to which is tacked an ore-dressing
plant and the rights of the patented process known as the
Banfield. The Junction South is a “ long shot.” It lies
south of the Junction ; but, then, the lode merely elbows
out in the Junction, so far as the workings show, so that
the best hope of the Junction South is the possession of a
semi-detached chimney like the one the J unction is working,
or the underlay of the main lode. But the diamond-drill-
holes on the Brisbane Blocks give little hope of the under-
lay being got at a workable depth in the claims running
along the eastern foot of the hill; while, as for the chances
of getting a semi-detached body, the claim is rather too
far south to benefit by the apparent break-up of the lode
in the British. Still, it is not a wholly hopeless block,
and the sulphide-dressing plant has some good features.

A becoming vein of modesty runs through the
wired reports re the Great Boulder’s deep-level develop-
ment. They broke “into massive gold, in which pieces
of virgin gold as large as hens’ eggs occur.” Only hens’
eggs ! Not emu eggs or swan eggs.

H. E. C. Robinson, of Wentworth Court,
Sydney, publishes a map of the Hannan’s belt of leases
at Kalgoorlie, W.A., compiled by C. W. Cropper from the
field-notes of Gladen and Manning, a local firm of mining
surveyors. Its style and accuracy leave nothing to desire.
* * m m
Several of the N. Dandalup (W.A.) gold-finds
were soon found to have been salted, and the revelation
has made some Perth men feel very sick. Amongst those
who jumped most violently on the man who first con-
demned the finds was H. Rhys-Jones, editor of Perth
Mining Journal. In a long interview, Rhys-Jones was
represented as saying that instead of there being no
stone at the claims like that shown in Perth, there were
thousands of tons of it—which was probably true, only
that the thousands of tons had been gilded. The inter-
view wound up with a doubt on the sanity of the
impeacher of the field. Francis Hart, who has gone com-
pany floating to England, must also feel ill when he
remembers how he described one claim, also amongst the
gift, as “ a mountain of gold.”

“ Watch progress at the Scotia mine, at Dundas. De-
velopments are, in my opinion, better than in any show
at the Norseman—which, after all, is not saying much.”

Kalgoorlie : “ It is expected that the next Kal-
goorlie escort will be the heaviest that has ever left the
town. Considerable feeling is being manifested at the
continued indifference to the request that the yields of the
fields should be given separately. All the gold of the
Kalgoorlie mines is credited to Coolgardie.” Having next
to no gold-record of its own, Coolgardie gets the benefit
of the outlying places. This enrages the Kalgoorlie edi-
tors, who have started to expose the barrenness of the
Coolgardie mines, and to express great sympathy thereat.
* * * *
Adelaide : ‘ ‘ Several large nuggets from the
Devon Consols claim, Black Flag, W.A., were on view to-
day. The largest weighs 3030 z. lOdwt.” They were found
last year, but the omission to say so gives them an air of
freshness.
* * m *
Great Boulder, 932 tons for 31650 z., and it is
thought that this grand average can be long maintained.
* * m *
Nannine, W.A. : “ The specimens shown from
Cocktree Flat are literally studded with gold in large
lumps. Numerous leases for the purpose of flotation have
been taken up.” Certainly-why not ? How is it possible
to work mines studded with gold in large lumps without
foreign capital ?
» * * *
Coolgardie —“ Tuesday—Shierlaw’s started at
Thursday last and has been running ever since.” Surely
not! —a battery to run five days is too good to be true.
How innocently the Coolgardie hummers give themselves
away ! The Empress of Coolgardie battery is reported to
be doing good work putting through small trial crushings
for different mines. How very consoling for the British
shareholders ?
* * * *
“ The famous Hill End mine at Broad Arrow
(W.A.) has been shut down, and Dr. Simon has left for
Paris.” This fact was not wired East. The stoppage is
put down to the expense of running a three-head battery.
All the miners, including the mining manager, have been
discharged.
* * * *
The disappointing Golden Zone crushing —less
than an ounce when over three was expected—is causing
some paper-war at Kalgoorlie. Of course it was “ all left
in the tailings,” which, strange to say, assayed higher
than the blanketings. The battery-manager now comes
along with a tailings assay showing that the extraction
was not so bad after all.
* * * •
From London to The Bulletin: “The W.A.
market has hardened a little lately, on account of the
good yields and reported strikes in the Great Boulder arid
Lake View; but, at the same time, there does not seem
much life in it, and there is very little real business doing.
They are expecting great things here from the Brown’s
Creek crushing. If that mine should prove good, it will
be a good thing for N.S.W. There are several strong
people talking of doing business in N.S.W., and you may
look for quite a demand for mining properties in your
country before long.”
* * * *
The British Australasian (published in Lon-
don) of Jan. 30 contains a big advt. of “the Bethanga
Goldfields, Limited, Victoria, New South Wales.” Then
it goes on to explain that the venture is situated at
Beechworth.
* * * *
Bendigo’s April calls, .£15,295, again run the
divs. close. Ballarat asks for £22,911. Past month’s
divs., £10,195.
A powerful agitation is afoot to force Vic.
Government to pass an Act whereby the Korrumburra
and adjacent coal-fields are to be put under similar regu-
lations to those provided for by the British legislature in
re the inspection of coal-mines. The Bulli disaster alone
should have stimulated Vic. Government to take some
precautions, but it is only the M.L. calamity that has
roused the Vic. public to the fact that a coal-mine is a
coal-mine wherever situated, and subject to sudden
disaster.
XT * * *
The Victorian coal output for the first three
months of ’96 was 49,747 tons—an increase of nearly
14,000 tons compared with the first quarter of ’94, and
4700 tons more than the first quarter of ’95. The industry
has paid £2812 in dividends during the quarter; it sup-
ports 945 men—an increase of 159 in the past year ; and it
already means £150,000 a year less sent out of the pro-
vince to pay for imports.
* * * *
An alleged silver-lead min®, with a lode 100 ft.
wide, yielding 69 per cent, lead and 9oz. silver to the ton,
is reported from Murind.il, Gippsiand. A syndicate has
leased 100 acres, and is wearing wet cloths round its
head to keep it from going mad with excitement in the
meantime.
* * » *
In this issue appears the prospectus of the South
Homeward Bound G.M. Co. v hise property of 30 acres is
situate at Queenstown, 27 miles from Melbourne. Queens-
town is a promising field which the average Melb. man
refuses to believe in mainly because it is close to his own
back door, while he yearns after the far-off wild-cats of
W.A. simply because the distant field is always green,
and the distant spec, looks like a 40oz. nugget. The co.
is in 30,000 shares of ss. each, so the risk is a very small
one.

£7OO in debt. Average yield, under 4dwt., hardly gets
within cooee of the ex/s. Mine improving in the 300, 400
and 500 ft. levels., and in a few months it should pay its

Great South Long Tunnel, Walhalla (Vic.),
with three years’ payable stone in sight and good pro-
spects of making other “ strikes,” now stands a chance of
returning some of the £36,250 sunk in its development.
For the bi-term 5080 tons for 39190 z. gave the first 6d. div.
and £3OOO reserve.

Democrat, Glen Wills’s (Vic.) solitary div. mine,
selling price to the Britishers ; £9600 cash and 14,400
paid-ups in a £lOO,OOO co. with £15,000 working capital.
Also within nine months the buyers have the option of
taking aforesaid shares at par.
* * * *
A scheme is on foot for providing electric-power
to run all the batteries about Sheep Station, Vic. By the
way, the Big Reef, Mt. Hepburn, in this district has been
worked off ini London, and the confiding Britishers are going
to do big things. The Dorothy claim, adjoining the High-
land Chief, has just changed hands, purchase-money
£lOOO. Men continue to drop in on the field, misled by
newspaper blatherskite, look about and drop out again
hurriedly. Small prophets, and quick returns.
* * * *
Chiltern Valley, Rutherglen (Vic.), pursues the
even tenor of its divs. Last half 3927 fathoms of wash-
dirt gave 45170 z., and paid £9OOO-grand total, £115,405.
Has nearly 12,000 fathoms ready for blocking, and a big
area of ground yet to work, so that more profits for a few
years seem pretty sure.
» * * *
Great Northern Extended, another Rutherglen
(Vic.) div. “daddy,” paid £6OOO to scrippers for the
six months. With the resumption of work in the leading
washdirt drives this term should pan out even better.

On the inside of front cover in this issue will be
found prospectus of the King of Croydon No. 1 North
Gold-mining Co. The co.’s property consists of 10 acres
at Croydon, N.Q. ; 1316 tons of quartz from this ground
have already been treated fora yield of 5811 oz. of gold, thus
the concern has a substantial reputation to back it as well
as a quantity of good reports. The capital is £15,000 in
60,000 ss. shares, so the venture starts without the crush-
ing over-capitalisation whn.ii has wrecked so many Wes-
tralian and other specs.
» « » *
Mt. Forrest p.c. (Monah-Gladstone district) sent
in the other day 2210 z. from 81 tons—the largest cake yet
taken from the Mt. Forrest line.
* * * *
Eidsvold returns for this quarter show an in-
crease of about 40oz. over the same quarter of last year.
The tonnage, however, is less—therefore, the stone is
richer. Total gold-yield up to March 31- 6630 z. 12dwt.
Sapphire areas at Anakie are attracting attention, and
Victorian speculators have an agent there.
* » * *
Reducing-operations at Burrum Beach (Mary-
borough, Q.), conducted by expert Canning, resulted in a
button of gold—about 4)40z. The usual bedding of
amalgam left on the plates was estimated at about 4oz. a
plate—altogether 18oz., a handsome return. According
to the prospectors, a man with plates can make about 7s.
a day, and an experienced miner about 18s. The only
trouble is that the plates get somewhat clogged with silver
(or platinum).
* * • *
Brunnerton colliery, wherein so many lives have
lately been sacrificed to carelessness, has had a chequered
career. It now consists of three mines, about eight miles
from Greymouth. Well on for £150,000 has been swamped
by private owners and by co’s in attempts to develop these
properties, and it is only within the last few years that
the combination of the Westport Coal Co. and the Union
S.S. Co. has caused fairly payable results. The men
worked with naked lights, and, as there were accumula-
tions of explosive gas in all the old workings, it seems
marvellous that no serious explosion occurred before the
present one. In 1893 there was a small one, but little
attention was paid to it. The Brunner editor is being
criminally prosecuted by the co. for his comments on the
catastrophe.
* * * *
After boring 1100 ft. and striking oil in Taranaki
(M.L.), the roof of the oil-seam fell in and stopped
supplies. Now the enthusiasts are going down to
1500 ft. What they will now hope for is a harder stratum,
so that the well won’t be blocked by a cave-in whenever
a tap is put on.
* » * »
Tasmania has Melb. speculators fairly by the
ear. About 30 silver and copper shows are having a
market flutter, and new gambles pop out daily. Buyers
will feel the dull thud ere long.
* * * *
Old Oceana, Zeehan (Tas.) resurrected into the
New Oceana, a Melb. co. with 100,000 ss. shares. The
“public” have taken up 50,000 at 25., and the vendors
lift the other half and £l5OO cash for a show worth little
or nothing three months ago.
* * * *
The coming Tasmanian Mt. Lyell law-suit is
causing much exploring of cobwebs by the Melb. legal
brigade. The status of co-adventurers "(commonly called
“mates”), the 4th and 7th sections of the Statute of
Frauds, the effect of the Statute of Limitations on trusts,
the legal difference between shares in a piece of land and
shares in a co. owning the land, the transmutations which
a legal right may undergo in its migrations through pros-
pecting parties, syndicates, and half-a-dozen companies,
and other such phantasmry of the law are all being relent-
lessly milled into costs. A strong Melbourne bar has been
retained at mouth-watering rates.
* * * *
A. G. Mitchell and Co., of the Strand, Sydney,
are placing “The Gold King Amalgamator” upon the
Australian mining market, and say some very strong
things about it. “ The Gold King is absolutely simple
and easy to work ; it has been crucially tried on all classes
of ore—clay, talc, talcose slate, black and beach sands,
and every time it proves itself “ The Missing Link” in
gold-saving devices ; it saves not only the fine gold, but
all the floured mercury. Hundreds of Gold Kings are
now working in California. The Gold King pays for its
cost in from 30 to 90 days as an equipment to a quartz-
mill. Where water is scarce, the machine can be run with
250 gallons per ton, and this water can be used over a
number of times if necessary. The power is nominal—
viz., from 1-12 h.p. for No. 0 to one h.p. for No. 2.”

Parkes’s death and burial —there were, un-
happily, some very squalid and unrecordable cir-
cumstances connected with the poor old man’s last,
days on earth—gave occasion for the display of
bathos and slobber with which the daily press in-
evitably marks such incidents. Sydney Tele-
okaph, which took the lead in howling Parkes out
of politics, wept gruesomely over “the foremost
man on this continent,” and printed execrable
pictures, in execrable taste, of the dead politician’s
bedroom, with live politicians attitudinising
around ; and of the bearers puffing under the
weight of the coffin. And in between its slabs of
gloom concerning the funeral, it paused to cheer-
fully remark on the scene at Penrith railway re-
freshment-room, when “ the famished mourners
speedily exhausted the stock of sandwiches and
cake.” The S.M. Herald reporter also spread
"TALKING OF TRILBY.”
HE : “ I quite agree with the author’s argument that ‘nothing is so chaste
as the nude figure.”
SHE: “Have you chased it much?”
nimself to great advantage, and noted regarding
the scene at the grave that
It was a beautiful afternoon, and the rugged mountain
scenery formed an admirable background to the mournful
picture. Inside the enclosure, by the open grave rested
the coffin, the polished wood and the silver trimmings
glittering in the sunshine. Nature seemed aglow : but
there was solemn silence—broken only by the reading of
the beautiful service of the Church of England for the
burial of the dead.
“Nature aglow,” providing “rugged mountain
scenery” as “an admirable background” (!) to
“ the coffin, the polished wood, and the silver
trimmings. ” Booh !
But it all goes down, apparently. The sense of
numor amongst daily-paper readers is strong enough
to swallow any anti-climax which isn’t definitoly
labelled a joke and put in the funny column. The
tears of the grieved ten-year-old, the sympathy of
the loathsome advertising wreath - sender, the
telegrams of alleged condolence from schoolboys
(per a pushing headmaster) and from the person at
ifatnberoo who established a particular claim to
get his little oar in on the ground that “ Kiama
was always Sir Henry’s favorite electorate ”
thanks to its “ghost,” no doubt; these things are
all absorbed by the person in the tram along
with the police reports. And Lady Parkes even
thanks Gov. Buxton for his grotesquely consola-
tory letter :
Dear Lady Parkes, I must write a short note to assure
you of my sincere sympathy in the bereavement which
has been permitted to fall on you, and no less in the
responsibility that you feel in reference to the young-
children. When I recently visited Sir Henry Parkes, he
told me how much of careful attention and affection you
had displayed towards them. I think you will now like
to know how much he-appreciated your care of them.
Well-meant, doubtless ; but what impertinence in
a stranger to assure a widow that he hoped she
would be comforted : her dead husband really-
appreciated her care of the children ! What
would Gov. Buxton say if somebody who met him
once took it on himself to assure his wife, whence
goes, that he was really very grateful to her “ for
her careful attention to the children ? ” Gov.
Buxton isn’t a humorous person ; but he would
probably see the incongruity in his own case.
* * * *
The Australian Star plumbed the depths
of bathos in its struggle to be elegiac over Parkes :
“ He died as a patriarch should, gathering his feet
up into the bed, and yielding the ghost Without
moan or sigh.” This assertion that the veteran
gathered up his feet off the floor or out of space is
construable as a reflection on the nurse.

* *
The recent S.A. Assembly elections pro-
vide food for reflection. It was confidently antici-
pated that the women would vote in one large
mass for the candidate with a spotless moral
character, ignoring the sensual person with six
wives, but in most cases the latter came out on
top. Similarly, the ladies were invited to
crucify the political Moloch with the pro-
fligate imagination and coarse vocabulary,” but
Moloch got in, as usual. And, despite vigorous
canvassing, the brewers’ nominee was elected
instead Mf the long, thin cold-tea man, simply
because the average S.A. elector much prefers to
swallow beer and temporarily feel like a king than
pour ginger-ale down his neck. Until the tee-
total party invent some drink which will not sit
like a brick on the sovereign elector's stomach its
chance at the polls is small indeed.

H. : “ Judge Hood, of Melbourne, de-
cidedly a good judge, has nevertheless been just
affronting my ideas. He had the familiar ‘ Let
him marry the girl ’ case up in court, after a very
bad fashion, the man bringing a girl of 15 into
trouble. The offender wanted to marry his victim,
but her father would not hear of it. Thereupon
the Judge is astounded, and cannot understand
such infatuation. What would he think of the
perverted mind of the girl who said to the Melb.
Police Court Bench : ‘ I will never marry the
man who deceived me. I only want the main-
tenance of his child.’”
* * * *
A Melb. bigamist got a rational sentence
from Justice Hood, the other day. It was a case
m which the second “wife” had always known
her position, and been properly supported by the
man who put her through a form of marriage,
bhe was the only person who could have been
offended against, and she wasn’t offended in the
least Therefore, the judge, instead of gratifying
the first wife s spite, let the prisoner go forth on
his own recognisance of .£5O. Scores of similar
bigamists have languished in limbo at the instance
of vindictive matrons who, as likely as not, had
refused to live with them aforetime. The vengeance
is chiefly directed against “ the other cat.”

The duties of an Agent-General are mani-
fold. A most fashionable “ bought-and-sold ”
marriage in Australian society was followed by
the usual trip to England, and the happy couple
domiciled as “ paying guests ” in a very high-class
Western boarding-house. Marriage had not,
however, curbed (say) Mrs. Jones’s skittish ness,
and as a result the “ paid ” hostess waited on the
representative official to enquire. She was assured
that they were wealthy, and that she need have
no fear about her account. But no, that was a
very trifling consideration to the hostess, her
fears were much more weighty, as her good name
was at stake : “Were they really married?” she
asked.

The Supreme Court of Kansas, U.S.A.,
has decided that a married woman need not take
her husband’s name unless she likes.
Last week, in its answer to a corres-
pondent, the Stab (Sydney) remarked, “The
policy of a contemporary is not a matter of public
interest. ”

The “ sticking-up ” of a Melbourne manu-
facturer last week was a humorous felony. Some
time before 8 o’clock in the evening the commer-
cial gent came bustling along a quiet street in
Richmond (Melb.) when a masked highwayman
put a pistol to his head and told him to turn-out
liis pockets. The demand was promptly met by a
production of £3 10s., which the traveller offered
to place in, the other fellow’s left hand. Suspect-
ing treacherous intent, the masquer (who was
probably quaking with terror) ordered his victim
to put the coin on the footpath and walk away,
and whilst the obedient boot-builder made nervous
tracks for the nearest police - station Barabbas
departed with the booty down a side turning.
There is really no hero to this story.
* * * *
“Shearer” tells The Bulletin this story :
“ While travelling in New England looking for
shearing we struck a very Irish old lady’s selection
and got work. My mate was a town-bred lad,
decently educated, and Mrs. Muldoon couldn’t
stand him at any price—everything he said or did
was wrong. One night at tea we were talking poli-
ties, when madame chipped in: it was a great
shame, she said, that she, a woman with a bit of pro-
perty, hadn’t a vote. ‘Then you think, Mrs. Mul-
doon,’ said my mate, ‘ that it’s desirable to extend
the franchise to women?’ Whereupon the old girl
flured-up furiously : * Oi’ll give you a shlap across
the mouth,’she said, ‘if ever agen you use that
filthy blackguard talk in front of my little gurrl.”
The trouble in the N.S.W. “ Irish ” Rifle
Corps is the difficulty of providing commissions
for the number of patriotic “ gintlemin ” who will
bestow their patronage on the “ ragement ” if they
are appointed officers. Another little difficulty is
that many “ big men ” who don’t want to directly
recognise the regiment are anxious to get a son
or two in if “a bit o’ rank ” can be given them.

One Supreme Court Judge is not going the
right way to become a favorite with the legal
profession, both branches of which complain of
(say it softly) his Honor’s manners—or want of
them—in court. In a country town lately, a
leading local solicitor entered court, sat down at
the bar-table, or rather —since the Attorneys’
SUNDAY-AT THE SHEDS.
HALF-TIME PARSON (who has arrived to hold service at the sheds):
“ Well, my good man, any o’ my flock here yet?”
ROUSEABOUT (from the Never-Never): “Don’t know; haven’t seen
any. What’s yer ear-mark?”
Right of Audience Act—the table set apart for
the use of the legal professions, and put his hat
down in front of him. “ Will you take your hat
off the table, Mr. Blank ? ” said the judge. This
is not a police-court! ” If his Honor continues to
cultivate this style, a now absent judge will find on
his return to Australia that he has a formidable
rival in his celebrated performance— how not to
behave on the bench. ”

The Adelaide doctors who wired to prevent
British medicos from taking the places at
Adelaide hospital which they refuse themselves,
furnish an object - lesson m caste - selfishness.
Because they had a row with Premier Kingston
they were apparently prepared to let the sickpoor
g 0 Without medical aid-or even to prevent them
from getting medical aid. The public are quite
prepared to promptly stamp out etiquette of that
sort, whatever the doctors’ justification in their
private squabbles.
* * • *
The other day The Bulletin remarked re.
the Down tragedy, at Sydney, “ As if any woman
suicided by cutting her throat! ” Since then it
would appear that two women, one in Melbourne
and one in Sydney, have cut their own throats.
This paper will be glad to have pointed out to it
any other authenticated cases of a similar nature
within the last 20 years.

A HOSPITAL SATURDAY SUGGESTIVE.
The latest re Sydney University under-
graduates’ strike. The other night their debating
club proposed discussing the removal of the com-
memoration from the University Hall to the Town
Hall. This the Senate promptly vetoed, where-
upon the obedient students chose as a most fitting
subject: “That the Caucasian race has degene-
rated.”

Habitud: “The atmosphere of Melb.
Public Library breaks down, in middle age, every
man who has been long there. I know of three
complete wrecks of such. Chief Librarian Bride
only resigned because of health, which was
affected almost from the first.’
During the progress of
the late squabble between
Sydney University Senate
and the Undergrads.’ Asso-
ciation, the association scored
once, at least. The vice-
ohancellor, who is an M.D.,
had ordered that the associa-
tion’s notice re Commemora-
tion should be taken down
within 24 hours. The order
not being obeyed, the v.-c.
sent for the president and se-
cretaries of the association.
“ I gave orders that that
notice was to be taken down
in 24 hours ! ” “ Don’t get
excited, doctor, ” said the pre-
sident, coolly, taking out his
watch, “there are quite four
minutes yet.”

The Maoriland census-
papers, just issued, show that
one old-established Irish grie-
vance has at last been re-
medied. The back of the
paper contains a list of an
imaginary household —that of
a certain Thompson—as an
example of how the particu-
lars should be filled in, and
for many years this list has
ended with the name of
“ Bridget Connelly, aged 23,
unmarried, domestic servant,
Roman Catholic, born in Ire-
land, can’t read or write.”
Almost all the provinces, m
the “ example ” on their cen-
sus-papers, also wind up with
an Irish slavey who can’t read
or write, and this general as-
sumption of Bridget’s ignor-
ance has roused such indigna-
tion that the Seddon Govern-
ment has now taken her off
the paper altogether, and re-
placed her with one Susan
Smith. Bridget, presumably,
lias gone out of the servant
“gal” business.

J * * * *
Nothing has lately been heard of the re-
consideration of the case of the Mount Rennie
prisoners. It is well-known that the late barrister
Healy was, at the time of his death, engaged in
reporting on the case for the alleged Minister for
Justice. But the nature of his report, if he made
a report, does not seem to be known. The absence
of Judge Windeyer affords a good opportunity for
the release of the survivors—who, like the hanged
boys, were not tried but lynched.
* * * *
The betting in London (writes a sour
Bulletin correspondent, whose letter this paper
prints as a warning) is about even that radical
Reeves, the new M.L. Agent-General, will be
muzzled with the infallible K.C.M.G.-ship. He
has had a long interview with Chamberlain, am l
according to the press, “ came away full of admira
tion for that statesman.” Pushful .Toe knows
well by experience how to deal with radical “colo-
nials, ” whose sound and fury in 999 cases out of
1000 disappears the moment they come into con-
tact with real swells. An introduction to a stray
countess, and a few invitations to “ the best
houses,” and the last state of the radical becomes
worse than the first —for wives love to be able to
put ‘ ‘ Lady ” on their visiting-cards. I bet that
Reeves is doomed. Meanwhile the departure of
his predecessor from office is much regretted, for
Perceval was a model Agent-General. Suave and
yet strong, he united the social charm of old Saul
Samuel with the official firmness of Playford.
The shipping firm of Huddart, Parker &
Co. has been troubled of late with a deranged pas-
senger. Said passenger, in a lucid interval,
shipped himself at Melbourne for Maoriland, but
on the voyage he became violently insane, and the
M.L. authorities refused to let him land unless the
shipping co. became responsible for his board,
lodging and medical attendance so long as he
remained incapable, which might be fifty years or
more. He was then brought to Sydney, but the
same difficulty arose there. An attempt was
made to return him to Victoria, but though that
province didn’t mind letting its lunatic out, it
drew the line at letting him in again, and he was
rejected. In short, the marine crank was offered
at a great reduction to all the provinces, and
declined. Then, finding it had either to carry
him back and forward and round about as a per-
manent affliction, and let him gibber on deck on
moonlight nights, or else become responsible for
his keep, the co. landed him at Melbourne, and
entered him as a permanent charge against the
profits. As a solitary item he doesn’t matter so
very much, but if this sort of thing happens often
the shipping go’s may take to dropping their
lunatic overboard on dark nights and saying
nothing about him.

Spagnoletti : “ There ought to be a half-
yearly gaol-clearance. This way : Every now and
again one hears of some well-known character
who has been let out of prison, prematurely, and
three times as many, probably more, are let out on
the sly. But how about the poor unknown men,
or even women ? I have seen a man get four years
for stealing a waistcoat. Another gets, possibly,
15 years for sacrilege, in stealing some tin-pots
from the conventicle where the Judge goes.”
Apropos, memo, for Vic. M.’sP. How about
the man who long: ago got 15 or 20 years for
sacrilege ? Is he still in gaol, or is he dead, or re-
leased ? And was he sentenced by a Judge of the
church against which the offence was committed ?

From a private letter from Menzies (“ 120
miles behind Coolgardie ”)“ .... Eggs cost
two shillings and half-a-crown each. Meat is Is.
per lb ; water, Is. per gal. To keep a house here
costs, on an average, £1 per day. Last week
potatoes were Is. per lb. This week there are
none to be had. There is no flour, consequently
no bread. ... Of the two, give me Hell.”
* * » *
In Charters Towers police-court. Com-
plainant, asked by solicitor for defence if he did
not threaten to “ take it out ” of his neighbors :
“ Yes, and I’d take it out of you —too.” Solicitor:
“O, no, you wouldn’t. There’s nobody in Charters
Towers can do that, I promise you. I can run.”
DEA EX MACHINA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Long years ago, ere legs were in the cast,
Or tanks, or trains, or women with a past,
The play bloomed bright;
To solve the complications he had wrought
The ancient dramatist bestowed no thought,
For, when things got much mixed in any scene,
A god swung from the flies in a machine
To put them right.
To-day, a helmless ship without a chart,
Life groans ’neath Ibsen drama, Beardsley Art;
The crank' looms great.
Buck to the chaos black from whence it came,
The world seems bound to shiver in its shame,
When—quick, with aspect confident and keen—
The goddess pedals out on her machine
To set things straight.
M‘G.

Sydney, May 5, 1896.
My Dear Moorabinda —
The latest Sydney society fad is the formation
of a cycling-club for ladies and gentlemen. It has
its rise at the barracks, where there is a greai
donning of bloomers thinly veiled in skirts, anc
mounting of wheels—for which the barracks gee
gee, that unwisely carries a lady, is, no doubt
truly grateful. Many a dashing militaire haulec
over the coals by Major-General Hutton for riding
a galled horse on parade will also bless the day the
Bicycle Club sets sail. But I don’t think thai
quite all the society damsels are joining in the
new club.
Apropos bloomers —though, to be honest, the
great original hideous knickerbocker is no more
like the ugly peg-top pantaloon tied in round the
ankles with absurd little bows of ribbon than
ballet-girl’s tights are like Bishop Smith’s gaiters.
Women may congratulate themselves on having
appropriated man’s nether garments in the teeth
of Lawnorder, but it’s only a false triumph, after
all, for a woman was arrested in Melbourne re-
cently on a charge of disorderly conduct because
she had trousers on.
There must be something sacred and unapproach-
able about pants that even the New Woman can-
not overcome or relegate to the limbo of exploded
fads. Though at first blush one would think that
a moral and virtuous Bench would be far more
shocked at Eve in knickers, which reveal so much
of her, than at Eve in discreet trousers lapping
well over her insteps, the inscrutable Law has de-
clared that a woman in trousers is disorderly,
while a female in knickerbockers is merely
ridiculous. Half a yard or so more of material
below the garter converts a comic cut to a breech
I mean a breach of the peace. How grotesquely
inconsistent! It looks as if the rational-dress
movement was actually tying the Law in a knot.
Miss May Mitchell’s piano recital at Sydney
Y.M.C.A., the other day, proclaimed itself an
amateur-show by the large audience, which had
girded up its complimentary ticket, so to speak—
otherwise, it looked very much like the ordinary
conventional concert. I found it pleasant, as a case
in point, to show how a talented pupil may perfect
herself in music without going ’Ome and getting
seasick or becoming engaged to the first mate.
Miss Mitchell displayed the true artistic tempera-
ment in her performance of several severe and
first-class items, and got in some fine effects in her
duets with her teacher, Mons. Kowalski. If she
doesn’t end up in the van of the long-haired
brigade, her debut'was one of the most misleading
events I ever witnessed.
A company of clever operatic amateurs, who
have no distinguishing appellation, played “ Pina-
fore ” at Sydney Gaiety Theatre the other evening
in aid of the North Sydney Hospital, which is
suffering from quite a church-like attack of lack
of funds. Lady Hampden was billed to attend,
but owing to the death of ex-Premier Parkes the
V.R. presence was withdrawn. When a local
big-wig dies it usually means a night or so off for
Guvmentouse, which only gets a holiday under
the shadow of grim Death. There was a large
attendance of sympathisers, and the operetta went
briskly, all the performers—save a new and very
successful Hebe—having won their spurs in it at
suburban representations. Miss Deakin, as
Josephine; Mrs. Imray, as Buttercup; Mr.
Beattie, as a fine-eyed Captain Corcoran ; and Mr.
Rainford as Deadeye, made the hits of the even-
ing, as they did before.
Apropos “ Pinafore,” it is one of Mr. Tither-
adge’s stories how he arrived in Sydney with the
only book of the words obtainable in Australia,
which he had bought in London for 6d. or so, and
which the play-pirates, who then infested these
shores, vainly offered him .£4O for. Needless to
say the virtuous mummer resisted the temptation.
The lawn-tennis contest began to leap at Sydney
Association Ground last Saturday, which was
also Hospital Saturday, a day when every
street is stuck -up by brigand philan-
thropists in petticoats, and most people stop at
home and sit on their small change as the only
way of keeping it safe. Consequently, there
wasn’t an alarming crowd at the tennis—though
the ball-and-racket question is always like a snow-
ball, gathering as it goes, so that the last state of
the gate is better than the first, and meridian
attendance is always reached when Her Ex. is
handing out plated butter-pots on the last day to
the exhausted victors. The match this year has
attracted Cornstalk, Gumsucker, and Bananaland
experts—one new champion entering the lists
bearing the, honeymoon-cum-nursery name of
“Diddams.” As I said, the tennis crowd wasn’t
a success on Saturday, and when a “ buster ”
acting in the special interests of the clothing-
trade, apparently—first smothered ’em in dust and
rubbed it well into the nap with a watering-pot
shower of rain, the woe-begone scatter put a suit-
able termination to a bald and unconvincing after-
noon.
Under the somewhat palpitating heading, “ Dis-
covery of a foreign nobleman,” the D.T. “ special
reporter ” recently tells how at some unheard-of
halting-place on Gov. Hampden’s recent ride to
Glen Innes, His Ex. was astonished at the per-
fection of some omelettes achieved by the shanty
cook, who, to cut a long story short, turned out to
be a high-toned political exile in the shape of a
Hungarian count making flap-jacks in a wayback
hostelry ! Gov. Hampden, with the overpowering
yearning to shake hands with everybody which
amounts to a mania in Governors, sought out the
count in his kitchen to thank him for his pancake,
and, also, to bowl him out for a barber, but the
count, “ an active old gentleman of 78,” proved to
be a bona-fide, and H.E. made the discovery that
he had known his brother, Prince Batthyany, in
London. But Gov. H. must get accustomed to
that sort of thing in Australia, where it’s ten to
one your horse is rubbed down by Lord Tomnoddy
come to the end of his tether. The Australian
bush is full of aristocrats banished for all sorts of
reasons, from politics to pocket-picking. Gov. H.
will presently discover that the toniest gathering
he could possibly arrange in Australia, so far as
Court Circular names go, would have its guests
recruited from the shearing-shed, the drovers’
camp, the stable-yard and the hotel-kitchen of this
large and woolly continent.
Gov. Hampden on tour has not been asked to
stand godfather to anybody’s bald, pink infant so
far—in which he has better luck in the North than
ex-Gov. Carington, who graciously allowed a
happy couple to call their bouncing boy, who
arrived during the Gov. ’s visit, ‘ ‘ Carington Blank, ”
and sent the infant a silver mug on his return to
town. To have a vice-regal godfather is one of
the most capricious streaks of luck, as some people
call it, that can befall an unconscious bush baby.
It has got to be born when the Governor is paying
a visit to its parents’ township, and, as these
things are difficult to arrange to the exact tick of
the watch, the chances that the Gov. or the baby
will arrive too soon or too subsequently for one
another make it a most open finish. Gov. Hamp-
den has only Fate to thank that his reckless
roamings (in the best interests, apparently, of the
Fresh Food and Ice Co.) haven’t led to a baker’s
dozen or so of little red “ Hampden ” Brown-
Joneses and Robinsons all calling him god-father
and waiting expectantly for silver sop-holders.
On dit that one of the latest potentates to
strike Australia is rapidly establishing a reputa-
tion for thrift that argues either a very stingy soul
or a sadly shallow pocket. The latest story goes
that H.E. assumes a pained expression in the face
of his tailor’s bill, and goes over the items in a
most disconcerting manner—remarking, “ I nevah
gave moah than (so much less) for my vests at
home,” or “Isn’t (such-and-such) an alarming price
for a pair of—aw—twousers ? ” Tailors can only
hope and pray that the vice-regal haggle won’t
catch on in the clubs.
It was over the maiden reception of this careful
potentate, by the way, that a Society damsel, on
her promotion, showed her bounce by writing to
Guvmentouse, setting forth that, as her fashion-
able wedding was billed for the same date, Her
Ex. would, of course, see the feasibility of post-
poning the Y.R. reception, “ as all the best people
will be at my wedding. ’ But the rudest part of
the whole thing was that the hide-bound damsel
wrote to an official, urging him to use his influ-
ence to secure the postponement, “as all the best
people,” etc. —the official in question not being
amongst her invited. Needless to say, the recep-
tion came off, and looked just as impressive as
usual.
“ Saved from the Sea ” is Bland Holt’s latest
novelty at Sydney Royal, and any youth who
aspires to be funny-man at a picnic-party is ad-
vised to go and earnestly regard the ever-popular
Bland Holt as he sends the plates skidding round
the table, sits in the salad, and steals the pigeon-
pie. Of course, it is highly improbable that the
aforesaid youth would be given the same license as
Billy Brown at a real picnic, but he might try the
effect, at any rate; the results would be interest-
ing to onlookers, proving how far the stage may
be imitated with safety by would-be comedians in
private life. The picnic interlude (set in a scene
refreshingly like the Leura Falls) is the brightest
in the play, Bland Holt’s rendering of two catchy
songs livening up the rather painful melodrama
immensely.
“ Saved from the Sea ” does not lend itself to a
great rush of clothing to the heroine, Miss Watson,
who makes an artistic fisherman’s lass in blue and
a scarlet kerchief—later in white muslin and
another scarlet touch, and thirdly in a snuff-
colored houppelunde and a funny little bonnet like
a limpet. Mrs. Bland Holt rollicked through the
perky part of Polly in becoming rustic costume,
and the scenery suggested Yere Foster. The only
property I didn’t understand happened in Baker’s
cottage for Cosgrove to get murdered in, and
looked like a cradle on end serving its time as a
chair while awaiting further family developments.
There was a large and cordial house, and the collec-
tion of fans in the dress-circle was interesting
Perhaps the oddest were a black-and-white snread-
eagle arrangement suggesting gridiron marks on a
pigeon’s wing, cheek by jowl with a three-cornered
plaid wind-raiser for all the world like a tartan
tart.
So far, I am told, the Brisbaneites see the
Lamingtons through rose-colored glasses A
theless? the lively daughter of a departcddynasty
is sadly missed in her capacity of so y

It was fortunate that the Factory Chris u
ball was held this year at Paddington Town Ha l,
for the slim, though fashionable, attendance would
have looked a mere row of pins in Sahara had tne
function come off in the Town Hall. I lie ba
room was principally decorated with the u
lines of flags, apparently hung out to dry and
never taken in, which are the unvarying adorn-
ment of the Paddington ball-room, augmented by
ferns and palms, and the cosy dais was tricked out
with roses. Lady Hampden (who peers at Society
through a double eye-glass, and makes a
buffer of her committee-bouquet in the vice-
regal lancers) sported precisely the same turn-out
she wore at the agricultural ball, augmented by a
large com. posy of roses tied wit 1 the Hampden
colors. By the way, I don’t think such towering
bouquets are the kindest things to present to Her
Ex., who must sometimes fee) sprained from the
effects of holding them straight. Miss Brand
wore complexion colors —pink and white and the
most swagger appearance in the V.R. party was
an importation in pink jauntily rigged out with
pink tulle fluffily bordered, and ropes of unmounted
pink roses. It was the one distinctly new turn-
out in an ocean of resurrections.
Mrs. Harvey (hon. sec.) wore black satin and
chiffon, slightly teased on the bodice with gold
embroidery, but the average dressing was not
brilliant, but distinctly Mondayish. Two damsels
in bluish and strawberry plush respectively looked
more like a pair of occasional chairs than anything
else ; a strawberry blonde accentuated her wara-
tah-like appearance by a pale blue gown ; and a
matron in green and pink, with an odd sort of
pink tab protruding at the back, looked like a
ballroom-edition of the famous nursery hero,
Giddy-Giddy-Gout. A very special menu-decorated
supper occurred at the right moment, and floor
and music were above reproach, but the attend-
ance was the smallest the G.F. Club ball has
scored so far.
The increased number of women in hot-cross-
bun badges, carting round the noisy collection-
boxes in Sydney on Hospital Saturday, looked as
if the memory of the wealthy husband recently
achieved by a Sydney belle, all through her pro-
specting for coins at the entrance to Sydney’s
cniefest hotel, has raised a fresh crop of enthusi-
asts in the good cause —the number of new faces
being positively amusing. The general consensus
of masculine opinion, so far as I heard, was that
the last collectresses were not so good-looking as
other batches, and there was a complaint lodged
that most of those who were pretty militated the
effect with veils. Hilda Spong made a success of
her stall, and is about the first actress to advertise
herself over the hospital-collecting biz.
Sydney has been more like a shambles than a
city of late—we have had each day our daily
murder—one of the latest loving husbands,
accused of forcibly transforming himself into a
widower, bearing the suggestive name of Love.
Mr. Love, who tried to shoot himself, is now in
custody, and his case may once more illustrate
that which has been so often written : “ Marriage
is the death of Love.”
Writes my Melb. correspondent.—
“ Melb.’s new Mayoress is holding a series of
weekly afternoon receptions in the Town Hall.
The first took place last Wednesday, when almost
the whole of female Melb. was jammed into the
narrow ‘Mayor’s’ room together with refresh-
ment-tables, scores of pot-plants, much furniture,
and several aldermen —this on a close autumn
afternoon with a sticky north wind blowing out-
side. Those ladies who rushed in to grab the
Mayoral hand and snatch a fearful cup of tea
when the door was opened at three o’clock were,
by the force of the crush, compelled to remain until
the tide of callers ceased at about 5.30, and were
finally removed in an almost fainting condition.
Mrs. strong, the Mayoress, is an elegant-looking
woman, with a sweetness of expression somewhat
rare in these days. She much resembles Alex-
andra of Wales, without ‘ our sweet princess’s ’
excessive and rather out-of-date girlishness and
was suitably and handsomely gowned in black
silk. I oik m the know ’ were much amused at
watching an unwitting individual endeavoring to
introduce the second wife of a divorcee to the
sister of his first. The icy glances exchanged
during the contretemps mercifully reduced the
temperature for yards round the indignant pair.

SPORTING NOTIONS .
Bi cycle champions will presently get divided
into two classes-those who can do without pacemakers
and those who can’t. Then the champion able to win
“on his own ” will challenge the other sort of fellow to
race him as runners and scullers race, with a clear course
before them. And the bike “authorities (which don’t
appear to exist, thus far) will have to fix the conditions
under which championships are to be decided
* * * *
Jack Carlton, of N. Melb. C.C., has about the
best 1895-6 Australian I u cricket bowling record: 2000
balls, 60 wickets ; average, iu.B runs.
* * * m
“Graham is suffering from rheumatism and
Trumble from a bad cold. During the voyage Jones
slipped and injured his arm.” The new Australian Eleven
has started the usual '.rst of unlucky circumstances that
“ militate against the success of the tour, as cricket
journalism puts it. - .
Harris’s show of racing against the trotter,
Osterley, on the cinder track at Richmond (Melb.), was a
miserable farce. The bikist tried one heat, finishing fully
100 yards behind the sulky, and then gave himself up as
a bad job. The' track was too rough, according to the
unreliable Britisher. Subsequently Martin, with a flying
start, beat Osterley’s time.
* * * *
Pursey Chirnside’s Australian Trotting Ass. is
as snobbish as an English “gentleman.” It advertised a
match between Mr. John Robertson’s Osterley and W.
Martin, champion bicyclist. Why should Robertsor
driving a pneumatic sulky be Mister any more than cyclist
Martin driving his pneumatic wheel ?
Melb. should be thankful for cycle-god Parsons’s
departure for one reason. You can’t walk up street with-
out meeting a photo, of J.W.P. in every other window,
labelled : “ Dear Sir,—l have found your 4d. bar yellow
soap matchless for the voice and complexion. J. W.
Parsons.” Or “ Dear Sir,—Prior to beating the Aus-
tralian mile-record, I wore a pair of your unparalleled
pants, and can testify to the advantage, &c. J. W.
Parsons.” Or “ Dear Sir,—Your prime chops at 7d. a lb.
kept me in rare form up to the date of my match with
Martin. J. W. Parsons.” It grows monotonous.
* * * *
Parsons has left Melbourne single. Mr. E. B.
Russell, father of Miss Belle Russell, desires to contradict
the statement that a marriage was -pending between his
daughter and the Victorian wheelman, and says that the
circulation of the story has given much annoyance.
* * * *
Such a big Melb. demand for old ordinary
bicycles (“ scrap-heaps,” lately selling for ss. each) that
they have actually risen 500 per cent, in price.
« * * *
About the biggest of Melbourne cyclers is
Wertheim, of sewing-machine fame, who rides one of his
own German bikes. Wertheim and barrister Leon, doing
a demi-semi-sprint together on a calm Sabbath morning,
are an important spectacle.
* * * *
A Christchurch (M.L.) firm has brought out a
chainless bike with quadrant pedal levers instead of
revolving ditto, which idea catches on with the girls.
* * * •
Melb. opening football matches aroused no
enthusiasm. The principal game, Melb. v. Essendon,
watched in comparative silence, indicated poor prospects
of present popularity. A fair game, no barracking and
execrable umpiring showed what a shadow of its former
self is a contest which, two years ago, would have
attracted every leather-lunged ruffian in Melb. Essendon
won by 9 to 5, and may be premiers this year.
* * * *
Rev. Harry Bryant, 8.A., the new vicar of St.
Paul’s, Burwood, Sydney, has joined the Wallaroo Foot-
ball Club, and intends to play during the coming season.

Now that Pompadour Corbett’s financial pro-
spects have been well gauged in Melbourne, he will likely
visit Australia. His agent is already on hand to make
sure of the dollars. Pomp, leaves his company in the
States, and will travel on the athletic racket. He ought
to draw. People are bound to step along to see a man
who can talk the rest of the fighting world blind, and jaw
for a year without stopping. Dooley is down for an eight-
round go with the ex-bank clerk. Should Mick happen to
accidently send the ’Merkan gas-meter out, the great Aus-
tralian bashing brigade would stand on its head for a
week. i

POLITICAL
POINTS
Jingoism—Jingoism, fruitless howling.
Queensland has one Railway Commis-
sioner, South Australia one, Victoria one, and
N.S. Wales three.
John Norton, as secretary of the N.S.W.
Protectionist party, in which position he is strongly
supported by Lyne, parries Crick’s ambition for
leadership.
Bible-reading is said to be compulsory in
at least one Vic. State school, where the head
teacher rounds up all Protestant pupils for scrip-
ture lesson during school hours.
If, as is prophesied, one of the Newcastle
colleries shortly introduces non-union labor whole-
sale, the Reid Ministry will find itself in the
tightest place it has yet occupied.
Let it be said to the credit of Parkes that
he denounced the despatch of the Soudan con-
tingent. Perhaps only jealousy of Dailey inspired
the denunciation ; still, he did denounce it.
By, it is said, the orders of the Arch-
bishop, 22 nuns went to the poll at North Adelaide
—in the interests of Glynn and Harrold, the Tory
candidates. The clergy follow the collection-plate.
Adelaide Quiz did some remarkably good
tipping at the S.A. election. The results are now
definitely known in 25 electorates ; in 17 of these
it tipped both winners, and in the other eight it
tipped one of the two winners each time.
An Argus heading:—“The Matabele
Rising. Estimated Death Roll. 1000 Matabele
Killed, 300 Whites Murdered.” That command-
ment must have been sub edited by Moses ; it was
meant to read : “ Thou shall not kill a Britisher.”
MORBIDLY IRRITABLE OVER IT.
MIRANDA: “ Pa, I 'want ”-
PA : “ No, you don't; you can't have it. I can't afford it. This beastly, infamous, impoverish-
ing Landaninkum Tax”-
MIRANDA: “I was about to say, pa, I want you to come in to dinner; the soup's getting cold."
At current rate of retrenchment N.S.W
Civil Service Board is estimated to save a tota
of .£200,000 a year. The coincidence here is that
12 months before the appointment of the Board
Premier Reid said the Service could bear retrench-
ment to the extent of =£200,000.
Chamberlain has announced that the “ in-
vitation ” to President Kruger to visit England
had been withdrawn. A royal invitation is a
command. What is a governmental one? At
any rate, it has been withdrawn—to save appear-
ances, after Kruger had declined it.
Mayor Ives boldlv condemns State inter-
ference with any of the pursuits of private enter-
prise. And yet, as Mayor, he affixes lus approving
seal to Sydney municipal by-laws, under which
cabmen and vanmen are penalised if they ask
more than municipally-specified rates for their
services.
Courier : “ The rising note of loyalty met
Lord Lamington in the North. None of the dis-
loyalists seemed to be anxious to start a Social
Democratic Republic.” And the North replied
the same week by sending in member after mem-
ber pledged to the principles of social democracy.
At the present rate of hearing the case
against Jameson at Bow-street police-court, his
conviction wouldn’t happen before the end of next
year. The Salisbury Government is on the look-
out for something—say the Pretoria trial of Col.
Frank Rhodes &, Co. —as an excuse for dropping
proceedings against Jameson.
Of what avail is the secrecy of the ballot
on a Queensland station where the sum-total of
votes recorded is two to three, which are counted
o i the premises almost immediately ? In the
United Kingdom the ballot-boxes from various
polling-places are mixed before the votes are
counted—a reform urgently needed in more than
one of the Australias.
A new order of knighthood has been
created by Her Gracious Majesty, to be called
“ The Victoria Order of Knighthood ” —R. V.O.K.
A reward for converted Republicans and saved
Democrats, of course :
Phonetically this will do —
R. V.O.K. will please a few.
With ease the poorest wit might joke
Upon that meaning word “ revoke.”
Tardy confession of Sydney D.T.—one of
the journals that whooped fantastically over the
still-unanswered cablegram from the Great Free-
trade Caucus of N.S.W. to President Cleveland,
when Yankeeland was represented as “turning
Freetrade ” :
America is Protectionist, and we think the antagonism
to British principles of trade too strong to be overcome.
Now, Col. Bell, let us hear you swear.
It is difficult to get away from an old
wheeze when once it has become imbedded in the
system. Within the last two months <£2,000,000
of British capital has gone to the Thames (M.L.),
and it keeps arriving faster and faster. At least
<£500,000 has gone to the West Coast, and the
representative of a German syndicate with another
<£2,000,000 to invest has just arrived with a view
to buying up local mines. And yet Capt. Russell,
leader of the Tory Opposition, started the election
campaign the other day with the usual statement
that the Socialistic policy of the Government was
“ driving capital out of the country.” That state-
ment has been made so often within the last four
years that it makes itself now.
Attorney-General Want, on his return from
Japan, found amongst the depositions awaiting
investigation in his office those connected with
the committal of a country medico for homicide.
The committal had taken place on the strength of
“telegrams” and “other written documents
from the Govt, analyst, who was not called upon
to give oral testimony. Mr. Want quickly found
that there was no evidence against the medico
and “threw out the bill.” How long was this
serious charge kept hanging over the doctor s head
through the absence of King Jack on a fishing ex-
cursion in Japan ?
The defeat of certain Labor representa-
tives was balm to the soul of Mathieson, the Q.
railway boss, who cordially detests the prying eyes
of democracy. About the shabbiest trick ever
done in Bananaland was the sending for the gold
railway-pass of Fisher (ex-M.L.A. for Gympie)
before the final result of the election was actually
declared.
Et tu , Brute! “ With all our assumption
of enlightenment and superiority, we are actually
becoming narrow, non-progressive, and conceited.
• . . We are so self-satisfied that we think we
make the best, finest, and most attractive articles
in the world. ... We practically fling our
goods at other people’s heads, and say—‘ Take
them or leave them, as you choose !’ ”—U.S.
Consul-General Bell, about his countrymen.
The worst - paid
and most improvident
set of workers in Aus-
tralasia are the Tas-
manians. The only
active labor organisa-
tion in the island is
the Zeehan branch of
the Miners’ Associa-
tion, and even that is
largely controlled by
the publican and store-
keeper “ honorary ”
members. The rail-
way employes have a
sort of union—merely
a benefit-fund and
at the end of every
year all the surplus
cash in hand is divi-
ded pro rata among
the members to pur-
chase a high - class
Christmas spree.
At a Melb. Meet-
ing W. D. Flynn
argued against
woman - suffrage on
the grounds that as
the final appeal must
always be to physical
force, and as women
were incapable of bear-
ing arms, they should
have no power of
directing or control-
ling the physical force
of the community by
their votes. To be
logical, Flynn, in his
argument of “no re-
sponsibility no
power,” must cancel
the votes of all men
incapable of bearing
arms, which is one
way of abolishing the
present Upper
Houses. Physical in-
capacity does not
postulate either a
desire or an aptitude
for dodging respon-
sibility. Anyway, the
new woman will cheer-
fully take the risk,
simply because she is,
after all, the same old
woman who has
always had to take
the risks of war
whether she wanted
to or not.
Melb. Age on Parkes :
Literature was not his forte ; but rather the manipula-
tion of men. Not being cursed with any strong convic-
tions, he became an Opportunist in the strongest sense oi
the term, and never during the long course of his public
career did he lose his tendency to count heads, althougn
he often counted wrong. . . . As an Opportunist, he
patted Federation on the back one day, and on the next
refused to take any steps to bring it to accomplishment.
... To compare Parkes with Higinbotham, M Culloch
or Graham Berry would be absurd. Those Victorian
statesmen had that element of political genius —initiative
in which Parkes was deficient.
The emphatic answer of the referendum to
the S.A. schismatics and Tories who wished to in-
troduce the turbulent elements of theology into
the State schools should be a stunner for the Vic.
piety agitators, who declare that the people are
anxious to blast up the existing system in order to
introduce the Bible, denominational grants, and
the apostles of cant. The Vic. system is fast
abolishing dissension, religious and racial, amongst
the younger generations, hence it must have
enemies in the parsons and politicians who
flourish on the internecine hatreds of the people.
Melb. Argus audaciously cited Parkes as
a sad example of the human suffering that comes
of Protection. “His early history was pathetic.
He grew up in ignorance ; he suffered from starva-
tion. He lived in those hard days when the High
Tariff party ruled England, and sought to bar her
off from customers and from outside abundance-
endeavored to make her a dear country for the
working man,” and so forth. To which might as
well be added—“ He died in straitened circum-
stances as soon as Reid’s Freetrade had
fairly begun to blast his adopted land.
“Lands” Carruthers (N.S.W.) says that
there is no more sincere champion of Australian
Federation than Premier Reid. Not two years
ago Reid was the champion opponent of the cause.
Half-a-dozen years ago, Damshicawgo Dibbs was
a red republican ; now he is an Imperial ennobblee,
Jingoing for loyal military fame. Half-a-dozen
years ago, Postmaster-General Joe Cook was a
miners’-union taader, a theorising Socialist, and a
suspected firebrand ; now, he is a member of the
God-fearing Government, ’ a mealy - mouthed,
well-groomed person of the nauseously Respect-
able order. Many other notable Australian in-
stances could be cited. See how time and
politics twist human nature, which is pretty muoh
of a muchness all round- the exceptions being the
cranks, who die in their own dirt and unshaven
chins. The modifications depend chiefly on a
man’s luck. If he “gets on,” he can’t resist his
surroundings. He would be false to “ No. 1” if
he did. “ Give me a fat sinecure,” is the average
mail’s religion.
The new New York excise law, which,
after a bitter struggle, has been passed by both
Houses of the Legislature, absolutely forbids the
selling on Sunday, either in saloons or clubs, of
even a bottle of beer with a dinner. Laws of that
sort are made to be broken. If a hundred decent
white men rent a building for club purposes they
can run that club for 50 years and in it wade up
to their necks in whisky on every day and night

A bill now before the British Parliament
poses to prohibit foreign sailors serving on
ritish ships unless they can pass an examination
in the English language. As Hans Jorgensen
and Knut Knutsen and Mohammed Ali generally
only learn their English on board, this will shut
out three-fourths of the foreigners, and it is in-
tended to do so. In other words, it is proposed
to protect the British sailor without saying so in
actual words ; the foreigner is to be barred out,
not because he is a foreigner, but on the ground
that he can’t understand quickly enough when he
is ordered to heave the binnacle overboard. It is
reported, bytheway, that out of 180,000 “1. ’tish”
sailors 160,000 are either Mahommed Ali or Hans
Jorgensen or Ah Sin, or words to that effect.
Britannia hires someone else to rule the waves
for her nowadays.
A “Madman’s Legacy”
“ Sire ! ” exclaimed a man in the plain garb of a
mechanic, to Richelieu, Prime Minister of France,
as he was entering his palace, “ Sire, I have made
a discovery which shall make rich and great the
nation which shall develope it. Sire, will you
give me an audience ? ”
Richelieu, constantly opportuned, finally ordered
the “ Madman ” to be imprisoned. Even in gaol he
did not desist from declaring his “delusion,” which
one day attracted the attention of a British noble-
man, who heard De Caux's story, and developed
his discovery of steam power. All great dis-
coveries are at first derided.
Nearly a quarter of a century ago an eminent,
wealthy business.man lay stricken with Bright’s
Disease. His physician said recovery was im-
possible. He used a new discovery, which, like
all advances in science, had been bitterly opposed
by the profession. Nevertheless it cured him.
The case was so prominent that the medicine was
at once subjected to severe tests by the most
eminent scientists, which resulted in its introduc-
tion into every part of the world. Such is the
brief history of Warner’s Safe Cure, which has
now, according to the testimony of eminent
persons, the most deserved reputation ever ac-
corded to any medicine, and its fame now belts
the globe.
What proof have we in our midst ? Ample ; of
which this is one:—Mr. Richard Peake, of
“ Peake’s Find,” West Australia, writes, “ I was a
martyr to deep-seated kidney disease. All treat-
ments and doctors tried failed, and Warner’s Safe
Cure cured me. Publish my experience that other
sufferers may read it.”

“ O Absalom, my son Ballington ! ”
Jack Lyons, the leviathan hitter, has got
married.
Alas ! Agent-General “ Tom ” Playford
has at last bought a Court suit.
Not generally known that Capt. Walling-
ton was offered and refused Melbourne town-clerk-
ship.
Donaldson and Parker are the only N.S.
Wales Premiers who can be said not to have died
poor.
The bush-rhymes of Ja.wbone Neild are
advertised as “on a par with ‘The Man from
Snowy River.’ ”
Dr. Pat. Moloney, of Collins-street, is sole
survivor of the first class of medical students at
Melb. University.
Of the 45 delegates who met at the Aus-
tralian Federal Convention in ’9l, Henry Parkes
is the sixth to die.
Treasurer Holder (S.A.) will be 46 on May
12; Chief Justice Madden will be 52, and Earl
Carington 53 on May 16.
There are now two Yankees in S.A.
Assembly, King O’Malley, insurance drummer,
and Jenkins, ex-book-agent.
During ex-Premier Parkes’s last election
contest (at Waverley) he was driven round in a
carriage provided by a local undertaker.
Dr. Harman Tarrant, late of Sydney, is
about to commence the practice of his profession
in London. He is beginning, says The Bulletin’s
correspondent, in a somewhat unassuming way in
the Edgeware-road, not far from the Marble Arch.
Melb. : “ The eyes of
patrons of concerts in Melb.
Town Hall are offended by a
series of life-size portraits of
past mayors, absolutely forgot-
ten persons, on the walls—
Smiths, Williamses, M‘Pher-
sons, Bennetts, Hodgsons, in-
clusive of people that not a single
soul in Melbourne can give the
slightest information about; but
there they are, with their stupid,
bourgeois mugs, embalmed in
