The ghost of Johnny Fawkner
might even say they were
labelled wrong —but I forgot,
they are supposed to be so
known that they don’t require
any names. ”
Dear Bulletin, The
“clever counsel” you mention
as vouching with his translations
for his sanity at the time he
made them can’t be very strong,
at all events, in medical juris-
prudence, or he would know
that perfect consecutiveness in
one direction is not, of itself,
any proof that the mind isn’t
hopelessly honeycombed in an-
other. A case perfectly in point,
and within my personal know-
ledge, was that of an excellent
all-round scholar, acute and
coherent in conversation and
particularly shrewd in literary
criticism, who was nevertheless
perfectly undisturbable in the
grotesque belief that he had a
monkey in his stomach ! It was
only when he began to explain
how such a thing was physically
possible that the “ crack ” be-
came apparent.— Speculum
Australia’s foremost biblio-
phile is a very wealthy Syd-
neyite, a non-practising bachelor
barrister, who has given his life
and devoted his income to the
accumulation of a now unique
and priceless library. No other
Australian possesses such a
hoard of literature concerning
Australia. The bibliophile lives
a solitary life among his beloved
books and pours out thousands
in adding to them, no trouble or
expense being too great for him
when in quest of a rare volume
or valuable MS Few people
have been privileged to inspect
his magnificent library, and he
much resents the intrusion of the
curious. It is related that when
Robert Louis Stevenson visited
Sydney he went to the collector’s
home, rang the bell, and sent in
his card. Presently the book-
sellers’ joy appeared. “I am
Robert Louis Stevenson,” said
the visitor. “And lam replied the
bibliophile, holding out his hand. “Allow me,
Mr. Stevenson, to wish you good morning.”
Queen Victoria, says the cable, has created
a new order of knighthood. It was quite time.
The old order was in a shocking state of disrepute.
One of the most curious cases The Bulle-
tin has heard of was that in which a Bananaland
Judge fined his associate £5 for falling asleep in
court.
Four out of six Vic. Supreme Court Judges
now cycle. The exceptions are Holroyd, who is too
old, and Madden, who regards the bike as undig-
nified.
Apropos Jack Monahan, the Melb. mil-
lionaire of not long ago. He began as a groom,
and got to own a lot of Swanston-street. His
panacea for longevity was a regular sea-bath, just
after dawn, at Kenny’s St. Kilda baths, where his
white pow and hard, spare, healthy mahogany
frame were familiar.
One of Kingston’s opponents for West
Adelaide is now in the lunatic asylum. “My
other opponent hasn’t even that excuse,” said the
genial Kingston.
There were but three University graduates
in last S.A. Assembly, and Ash (Kingston’s
partner) is the only one of these who has survived
the general election.
Gov. Duff’s predecessor at Sydney—what
was the name of that amiable earl ?—omitted to
send a wreath for Parkes’s coffin. However, he
will doubtless make amends with a cheque for the
widow and family.
The Shah of Persia has just been shot dead
by a religious fanatic. The modem potentate has
all the ill-luck of gunpowder, as compared with
dagger-times. The most energetic policeman can’t
well prevent a bullet from finding its billet.
The Argus gives John Cash Neild’s “Songs
’neath the Southern Cross ” a far more laudatory
notice than its satellite, the Australasian, gave
Lawson’s “In the Days when the World was
Wide. ’ The Argus itself altogether ignored the
latter.
“Madame” Midas, The Bulletin hears,
is still in London, whither her father, contractor
Cornwell, lately proceeded with the balance —some
GOOOa.—of the Dowling Forest Estate, adjoining
the celebrated Midas mine of nugget-specimen
notoriety.
Max O’ltell has been dishing-up our most
venerable Australian political joke as a little thing
of his own discovering. The cherished Larry
Bourke incident, “ Have anny other gintlemahn
any kwistions to ask?” is said by Max to have
occurred at one of the Redmonds’ meetings in
Sydney. This French collector’s jokes are rarely
arctueologioally correct.

The death of Parkes leaves Archibald
Michie, Q.C., of Melbourne, the last survivor of
the speakers at the anti-transportation meeting,
and the meeting to protest against the landing of
the convicts ex ship Hashemy, then lying in
Sydney harbour, in 1849.
It should be counted for righteousness to
the somewhat uncertain B. R. Wise that of all the
political pals whose public importance Parkes
more or less made, Wise was the only one who
stuck to the old man fallen upon evil days and
evil tongues. Some of the others have been think-
ing to make it up with tearful slobber over the
veteran’s coffin, but it shouldn’t wash. “ Give me
the friend who sticks to me when I’m wrong,” said
old Jack Robertson.

A Judge of Quarter Sessions, notorious for
his severity, not long since inflicted a very heavy
sentence for a trifling offence. The unfortunate
prisoner looked at him awhile in amazement,
then, more in sorrow than in anger, he said to?
Judge: “You cruel old ! an d walked
quietly down to the cell.
Of the Martin Ministry as formed in 1866,
the first in which Henry Parkes held office, no
member is now alive. A. A. P. Tighe, who held
office for four weeks preceding its fall, still lives.
Of all the Premiers of N.S.W. since the advent
of Responsible Government but three remain m
the flesh—Jennings, Dibbs and Reid.
Melb. demise : Fook Shing, ex-Chinese
detective. He returned to China, but came back
again here, apparently because of a mistaken im-
pression in the polite circles of Foo Chow that he
was an informer, whereas he was purely a salaried
public official. Fook’s weakest point was that he
gambled like an American minstrel, and whenever
the fan-tan prosecutions came on they rubbed it
into him, from Aspinall down to Gillot. But a
Chinaman seems to turn to wood in middle life,
becoming an animated relative to the renowned
Soo Chow forest of cinnamon-trees, south of the
clouds.
The N.S.W. Public Service Commis-
sioners, in the course of their travels, reached a
certain Government office and asked the head of
the department if he could suggest any method of
retrenchment. “Well,” he said, “I think a
beginning might be made by dismissing two of
you fellows. One can easily do the work. ”
It is printed that N.S.W. Police Inspector-
General Fosbery is to be succeeded by Col Wil
hams, now chief of N.S.W. military medical staff.
Should Mr. Fosbery retire, it would probably be
hard to find a more energetic or capable chief than
Or. Wilfiams, a born administrator who in a
wider sphere, would have climbed very high.

Parkes’s creditable achievements as an
aged parent were put into the shade by the
late Lord Tollemache, who, at SI years of age,
had a boy of 15 playing in the Eton eleven.
“Look at the power he put into t.iat ‘ off drive
and I was CG when the young scamp was born !”
the uxorious parent would say. He had chuckled
in his time over 23 new sons and a few daughters.
Parkes was Parkes to the last. When the
medical consultation took place, Dr. Money was
introduced. “ This is Dr. Angel Money, Sir
Henry,” they said. The dying old man looked
into the face of the keen-eyed physician : “ Dr.
Angel Money - you don’t look very much like an
angel, doctor.” When Dr. Ren wick called, the
veteran was too weak to say more th m : “ M y
old friend of forty, years! ”
During the conduct of a trial arising out
of a celebrated poisoning case, hold in Sydney
some time ago, counsel for accused would persist
in referring to the prisoner’s previous trial for
poisoning. This was not approved of by his
client, who from the dock sent the following note
to his attorney: “For God’s sake, keep X. off
that poisoning. ” After-events showed that prisoner
had excellent reason for his action.
The obituary article on Parkes, published
by The Argus, was mainly written years ago, nd
had since been twice brought up to date. The
man who first wrote it and the two who had to
bring it up to date are now dead ; and the man who
had to finally prepare it for publication is wonder-
ing when his turn will come. The backbone of
the S.M. Herald’s Parkes biography was
written fifteen years ago.
Maxwell, the coming Melb. advocate, has a
phiz like a common meat-axe. In the Mirams
libel-case he discoursed lengthily on “ face values.”
“ What right,” cried Purves for defendants, “has
my learned friend, with his physiognomy, to talk
about ‘ face value ’ ? What is face value ? ”
“ Cheek,” replied Maxwell, with an accent infer-
ring that his learned friend’s “ face value ” was
fabulous. Purves looked enlightened.
The octogenarian foreign nobleman now
cooking at a little Dalmorton (N.S.W.) pub., to
whom Gov. Hampden’s attention was drawn by
the surprising excellence of his omelettes, is
Count Carlo Casimir Vladislaus Batthyany,
brother of the well-known European turf-man of
that surname. He is described by the Governor
as “ an old gentleman of most polished manners,
whose brother I well knew in London. ” The Count
is quite happy so long as he can find somebody to
eat his omelettes and say they are good.

It According to N.S.W. Minister Carruthers,
technical and agricultural education along with
land for the people and freedom of exchange and
production must mean a glorious future for the
country. Quite so. When is it coming? Why
u'uu t it come before ? Is there any country
which hasn t been going to have a glorious future
any time within the last five centuries ? Is there
any country which isn’t still looking forward to a
glorious future ? Is there any country where the
glorious future has really arrived? What is a
glorious future, so that the country can recognise
it when it comes ; will Minister Carruthers de-
scribe it or explain what it is made of? Is the
glorious future going to be something like the
present, only more of it? About the five most
indefinite things in this world are the “ Anglo-
Saxon destiny,” and the “glorious future,” and
sound constitutional government,” and “com-
mercial and industrial freedom,” and the “crim-
son thread of kinship,” and this disreputable red
paper asks for an explanation.

“The Kitson scandal,” concerning which a
vague cable reached Australia the other day,
turned on the indiscretion of a doctor. Mrs.
Kitson met her husband in Sydney. His brother
is Sir Jarnes Kitson, head of the great breeds firm
of locomotive engineers ; and, when he took his
wife to England, his family at first showed her
every kindness. Then Arthur Kitson, the hus-
band, came out again to Australia, leaving his
wife, who was in poor health, in lodgings at
Bournenmouth. Dr. Playfair, who married a Miss
Kitson, has a world-wide reputation as an obstetric
physician, and Mrs. Kitson naturally consulted
him—some time after her husband hud left Eng-
land. He formed the suspicion that she was
enceinte ; and blurted it to Sir James Kitson, with
the result that things were made very miserable
indeed for Mrs. Arthur. Her husband came home
post-haste from Port Darwin, satisfied himself of
her innocence, and supported her in an action for
damages against Dr. Playfair. A jury gave her
<£12,000. Dr. Playfair, whose action throughout,
and especially in committing a breach of profes-
sional confidence, came in for keen criticism,
professed still to believe that his suspicion was
well-founded, but did not attempt to piove it.
Other evidence tended to show that Dr. Playfair
was decidedly wrong, and public sympathy went
strongly with Mrs. Kitson, who had been harassed
almost out of her senses.
The N.S.W. Fresh Food and Ice Co.’s
luncheon at Grafton, consequent on the opening of
the finest creamery in the province, was the biggest
success of its kind that ever took place in Austral-
asia. When the doors opened, some 600 farmers
and others poured into the large hall, and, almost
instantly, there began a great and joyous hum,
accentuated by the continual popping of corks,
and quickly giving way to the deep and sonorous
whirr of multitudinous jaws having a rare good
time. After the multitude had been fed, the
speeches began, and those reached their climax
in the vigorous and concise oration of Mr.
Pateson, the big co. ’s manager. The moment he
stood up he received a deafening ovation, far
louder and more prolonged than that accorded the
Governor and the other speakers put together.
And his speech and the speech of Mr. Mort (the
co.’s chairman) exhausted all that was worth say-
ing from a practical standpoint. Gov. Hampden
was much taken by Mr. Pateson’s speech : “ I
could listen to you till to-morrow morning, Mr.
Pateson,” he said. Of course the ex-politician
came along with his feeble and unnecessary echo
of what had already been said, and sat down with
an air of triumph after getting the usual parlia-
mentary patter off his chest, pathless, the crowd
was so happy with the luncheon and the Fresh
Food representatives’ speeches that it endured
these inflictions of words with the greatest good
humor. The Fresh Food Co. has expended
.£ll,OOO in its new buildings at Grafton ; the new
factory will make three tons of butter per day,
an 1, being right on the river’s bank, Half a ton of
butter can be put from the factory into the cold
oh amber of a vessel in two or three minutes.
Some distance out of Grafton the co. has erected a
large slaughter-house, to do business in beef and
pork, and it will also show the local farmer how
to make bacon.

Prospectus of the South Homeward Bound Gold-mining Co. No Liability.
(To be Registered under the Companies Act, 1890.)
OKTB TREE HILL, near QXJEEKTSTOWW, VICTORIA.. CAPITAL I £.7500, IN 30,000 SHARES AT 5 EACH.
5000 fully paid-up to go to the vendors in part payment, and 25,000 are now offered to the public, on which Is. per share is to be paid on application and the balance as required. =£soo will be placed to the
credit of the company free of all charges. It is fully expected that the money to be placed to the credit of the company will be sufficient to carry on its operations until payable gold is struck.
PROVISIONAL DIRECTORS : H. Dodds, Esq., Director Broken Hill South S.M. Co. ; Alex. Campbell, Esq., Director Block 10 S.M. Co. ; Thos. Learmonth, Esq., J.P. ; R. Pettigrew, Esq. : G.
Gorrie, Esq. MANAGER (pro tem.) : Robert Wallace, The Exchange, 369 Collins-street, Melbourne. BROKERS : Ellison & Evered, 371 Collins-street, Melbourne.
THE property is situated in close proximity to
that held by Messrs. Joyce, Hopkinson and
00. (three miners’ rights), from which extraordin-
ary yields of gold have recently been obtained,
and on which the following reports have been
made by the Government geologist, Mr. Reginald
Murray, as per the Argus of January 21, 1896;
also by Mr. T. H. Smith and Mr. R. Pettigrew,
who have both inspected the property.
“ THE QUEENSTOWN REEFS.
“ A report has been furnished by Mr. Reginald
Murray, the Government geologist, to the Minister
of Mines on his recent visit to the Homeward
Bound claim at One Tree Hill, near Queenstown,
in the Plenty River district. He states that the
specimens brought in a pocket-handkerchief to the
Mines Department last week by Mr. Joyce, one
of the owners of the mine, and which he (Mr.
Murray) predicted would yield lOOoz. of gold, had
yielded 1670 z. He writes that the gold is not
merely in specks but in absolute lumps, held
together by quartz, and ‘ in all my experience I do
not remember having seen anything richer.’ The
workings where the gold was obtained are about
30ft. from the surface, and the reef varies from a
few inches in thickness to 2ft., having a north and
south strike underlying to the east. He concludes
the report by stating that active mining operations
are in progress in the district.”— Argus, 31/1/96.
“Mr. R. Murray, the Government geologist,
recently visited Queenstown, and inspected Joyce
and party’s claim at One Tree Hill. He was much
impressed with the appearance of the mine, the
richness of which is indicated by the fact that out
of one patch, 30ft. by 20ft. in extent, the lucky
prospectors have obtained no less than IOOOoz. of
gold.”—Age, 20/1/96.
The property spoken of in this prospectus is
situated about three miles from Queenstown, 27
miles from Melbourne, and adjoins the ‘ ‘ Home-
ward Bound ” on the south.
The lease comprises 30 acres. There are four
reefs running through the property.
No. 1 reef is 4|ft. wide, with good walls.
No. 2 reef is 2ft. 7in. wide, with good walls.
These two reefs have been worked out to the 150 ft.
level by the Homeward Bound, and upon inquiry
at the mine I found that it has been worked by a
party of four men, and that the returns gave from
Boz. to lOdwt. per ton. Even the latter result
would pay handsomely. They are still working
these reefs with payable results. There was an
eight-stamp mill crushing while I was there.
No. 3 reef is not opened.
No. 4 reef runs through the property. This is
the reef where the rich gold has been got lately.
It is from 4in. to 18in. wide. It is not opened on
this block of ground. A tunnel run in from a
point in the creek would cut the big reef at 26ft.,
and No. 2 reef at 41ft., and No. 3 reef at about
85ft., and No. 4 reef at 150 ft.
When asked to take shares in this mine, I said I
would not do so until I had inspected the property
myself. I did so, and have taken up a large
number of shares, and have been asked to become
a director.
I have no hesitation in recommending this as a
good investment, and I believe it will develop into
a first-class paying mine.
I cannot understand why a valuable mine of this
description has been left so long without being
opened, and it so near Melbourne.
R. PETTIGREW.
T. H. SMITH’S REPORT.
On the 30th ult. I visited the Queenstown gold-
field and made a careful inspection of the various
mines being worked, the first one visited being
Joyce and party’s claim at One-tree Hill, known
as the “Homeward Bound.” This party hold
three ordinary claims, giving them 180 ft. along
the line of reef. Two shafts have been sunk, and
in both the reef is being followed down. The
south shaft is 40ft. in depth, and, in the drive
north and south, the reef measures from 6in. to
18in. thick, and shows good gold throughout the
stone, while occasionally sensational patches are
got. About 20 tons of stone have been crushed
from this mine, giving the magnificent return of
5040 z. gold, worth over £4 per oz. Good stone is
still being raised, and the reef underfoot appears
very strong, and gives every indication of proving
permanent, the wall from the surface being hard
and clean. The reef has a slight underlay to the
east, and runs north and south.
Adjoining Joyce’s claim is an ordinary claim
held by Hartley and party. Here the reef has
also been found, and. a crushing is now being
raised, the stone being exactly the same as that in
Joyce’s claim. The next property is lease held by
Wright and King, and known as the “ South
Homeward Bound.’ 1 This lease contains about 30
acres, and Joyce s reef can be traced right into this
property, the reef running along the crown of the
hill. The hill is about 209 ft. in height, and offers
splendid facilities for mining, as a tunnel can be
driven from the eastern wall of the hill, from which
four different reefs can be worked. The first
one met with is known as the big reef. This
reef can easily be traced the entire length of the
lease, and is a true fissure reef running parallel
with Joyce’s reef, viz., north and south, and going
down almost vertical. The reef is from 3ft. to sft.
thick, lying between two clean and well-defined
walls. In one place an open cut remains, showing
where the reef was worked over 20 years ago.
From this place the stone yielded as high as 7oz.
to the ton. In the south end of this cut the reef
can be seen measuring fully 4ft. in thickness. On
the south end of the lease a shaft was sunk in the
early days to a depth of 40ft., and a quantity of
stone taken out and crushed which gave very, high
returns, but the owners had only the primitive
windlass to work with, and were unable to cope
with the water. This shaft could easily be cleaned
out and retimbered, and a large quantity Of stone
taken out for crushing at a very low cost, and I
think a whip and horse could easily beat the water
for a depth of nearly 100 ft. The second reef met
with in the tunnel is the “Moonlight,” which also
gave very rich yields when previously worked ;
and the third one is the rich reef now being so
profitably worked by Joyce and party, and which
has given the splendid yield of 250 z. per ton for 20
tons of stone.
Running along the eastern boundary of the lease
is Femtree dully, the whole length of which
yielded very rich gold in the early days of the
field, nuggets of over lOOoz. having been got in the
creek, and as the big reef can be easily traced
running parallel with, and only a few yards from,
this creek, it is reasonable to suppose that this
rich alluvial gold was shed from the big reef. At
the mouth of the shaft sunk on the big reef some
very nice-looking stone is to be seen carrying iron
pyrites and galena—sure indications of payable
country. Another reef worked in the early days
on this lease is known as the Swedish Reef, and
from this reef very rich returns were obtained, and
it is safe to assume that this reef would also be
met with in the tunnel. A splendid site can be
obtained for a battery on the lease, and a dam
could be easily constructed to supply water for
crushing purposes, while an abundant supply of
timber for all purposes can be got on the mine. In
conclu ion, I have no hesitation in stating that the
big reef is the main line of reef in the Queenstown
district, and firmly believe that it will be profit-
ably worked to very great depths, as with the
many advantages of cheap mining that are to be
had in this district a very low average can be
made to pay.
In conclusion, I may state that after many
years’ experience in mining in the different colo-
nies, I have never seen such a splendid property
lying idle, and can safely say that if such a pro-
perty were in the remote parts of North Queens-
land or Western Australia, where the expenses of
working would be so very much greater, this pro-
perty would not be allowed to remain unde-
veloped.—Yours faithfully, T. H. SMITH.

for, early application will be necessary, as all shares
will be allotted strictly according to priority.

XTOTICE is hereby given that it is the intention of
BENJAMIN CRISPIN SIMPSON, of Sydney, in the
colony of New South Wales, Member of the Institute of
Civil Engineers, Esquire, to apply to the Parliament of
New South Wjles during the next session for a Bill to
authorise the said BENJAMIN CRISPIN SIMPSON, or
his Assigns, to construct a Bridge and Approaches thereto
between the South and North Shores of Port Jackson, the
said Bridge and Approaches to commence at a point in
Cumberland-street, in the city of Sydney, about thirty-
three chains fifty links north from the intersection of
Cumberland-street with Charlotte-place, in the city of
Sydney to Dawes Point, thence to and crossing the Waters
of Port Jackson to Milson’s Point, North Sydney, thence
to and terminating at a point in Alfred-street, about ten
chains fifty links north of the intersection of Bind-street
and the said Alfred-street, in the borough of North
Sydney ; to authorise the building over and diversion of
streets or roads; to authorise the appropriation or use of
certain lands required for or in connection with the carry-
in'-- out of the said work ; to make provision for compen-
satin# the owners of and others interested in such lands ,
to authorise the imposition of tolls on persons using the
said bridge : to confer powers for the making and enforce-
ment of bylaws ; and for all other purposes which may be
incidental thereto. Dated at Sydney this 13th day of
A 'LEONARD GIBSON, 92 Pitt-street, Sydney, Solicitor
or the Bill.

Bookies are practising their early tug-catching
game as usual. People in adjacent provinces are circular-
ised ; a forecast given of what will be nominated for Melb.
Oup, and the odds stated against each. Most of the
mentioned neddies are either being used as hacks, turned
out, or dead. Some mugs, “ honored ” with a personal
letter, fall in. Innocents are always abroad.
* * * *
The shocking display made by Duke of Rich-
mond in S.A.J.C. St. Leger disconcerted his new owner,
who gave £5OO for him. He was over 20 lengths off The
Merry Boy. The colt is brother to Lord Richmond and
Broken Hill.
* » * *
That first-class sprinter, Mostyn, failed to
annex S.A.J.C. Goodwood six furlongs for the third suc-
cessive year. Tinstream, the winner, who outheaded
O’Shannassy’s Homespun, is local bred, and managed to
keep the foreigners out. She started third top-weight
and paid a surprise div. of £l9 odd. In six years she has
scored 16 times, and got 27 places.
* » » *
The ,£l9 10s. tote div. on Tinstream’s Good-
wood win would have been an Ikey Mo 6 to 1.
* * ft *
Warpaint’s dead-heat with Hova (he lost the
decider) in Elder Stakes, under equal weights, shows
what a notoher he is when fit. As a four-year-old, he
dead-heated his rival for second behind the sensational
Titan in V.R.O. (Nov.) Railway Handicap, conceding the
now champion 221 b.
How fortune rings in her changes on lucky
stables ! On top of Wallace’s bruised heel came the lame-
ness of Challenger and Pilatus on the eve of their Mor-
phetville engagements. The fine Challenger was favorite
for the Goodwood, and the ring didn’t miss him when
scratched.
A couple of moneyed Maorilanders have secured
a fair heap about Tire for next Derby and Cup. This tear-
away youngster belongs to a galloping family, hut, apart
from that fact, Spring classic probabilities can’t be well
gauged, in any case, until after the cold season.
Dan. O’Brien again retiring from the turf. A
familiar yarn. Dan., though, must be sick of entering
waywardprads merely to make up fields.
Australian chess-champion Wallace (whose por-
trait recently appeared in The Bulletin on the occasion
of his defeat of Esling) beat Hodgson, the Victorian, in a
contest played at Sydney, by 7 games to I, with 1 drawn.
» * * »
A glance at the Royal Stakes and Birthday Cup
imposts shows a tendency to construct on the flattering
principle. Several apparently turned-loose neddies figure
in both. So far as the Cup is concerned, a great deal will,
as usual, depend upon sweep men coming up with the
stable bit to nix. “No whack, no jig.” Under ordinary
circs., Valiant, Fort, True Blue, and Gaulus seemed en-
dowed with winning chances, and if a light-weight scores
it will probably be the unlucky 6.10 Cradle, who is quite
capable of rocking over a mile and a half.
Dear Bulletin, — “ The tote never asks for odds
on but takes your money and pays a div.” Although the
tote always pays a div. yet the result is often odds-on. At
Onkaparinga, Disparity, winner of the Hill Railway Handi-
cap, paid £1 18s. div., i.e., started at 10 to 9 on. Numer-
ous similar instances are quotable.—Trim.
* * *
A weekly writer wonders why the Duke of Port-
land, and other big Hinglish owners interested in Aus.
turf, don’t send out some of their prads. When Melb.
Cup is ten thou, again, and bookies guarantee not to go
“ stony,” the Duke and Co. might be tempted. Cash
plums always entice people who don’t need ’em.
# * * *
There are stacks of money in the surreptitiously-
run Melb. “totes.” Last week two men were fined £lOO
for running one in Collingwood. That tote has already
paid £4OO in fines. It seetns to be squarely conducted,
has a wide-spread, clean reputation, and men, women
and children tramp miles to put on their sixpences.
» » » *
Coolgardie expects to entice Adelaide and Perth
neddies with a £lOOO meet. The one-time camel-race and
well-worn Publicans’ Purse are omitted from the pro-
gramme.
* * * *
The St. Albans double seems to be the favorite
public pick for all big meets. And it will likely be more
fashionable than ever at Derby and Cup-time.
* * * *
Horse-reporters talk of Auraria suffering the
fate of several previous Cup winners that did nothing
afterwards. But the mare has already followed up her
Cup victory by capturing the V.R.C. Oaks, and dividing
a stake with Wallace. Patron, Tarcoola, Glenloth and
Malvolio didn’t score one win after taking the big stake.
* * * *
The M.L. amateur walker, A. Brady, has re-
duced the 2-mile record to 14 min. 12 3-sth sec., or 54 2-sth
sec. better than previous best time.
* » * *
A Melb. small-course owner is imitating Caul-
field Oakleigh Plate and Purse divided-handicap idea. A
fair scheme for separating the sheep from the goats. How
would a divided Melb. Cup go ? Run the field in three
sets—first-class, medium, and cronk or blocking crowd.
By way of diversion the three in each could race off
for the total place money. Ridiculous, of course. But
the best neddy would have a much better show than here-
tofore, unless Ikey Mo winded him with a shot-gun as he
was coining up the straight.
The keen interest in racing manifested by Gov.
Hampden has set people wondering w'hy he doesn’t flash
colors on a neddy of his own. “ Guv’ment Brand ”
wouldn’t be a bad name for it.
* * * *
A “lydies”’ cricket-match at Narracoorte(S.A.)
was watched by two anxious husbands, who barracked
loyally while nursing bawling babies.
Says the New York World’s London corres-
pondent : —“ Almost any afternoon, between 4 and 5
o’clock, may be seen walking down the Strand the wreck
of what was once one of the greatest fighting-men the
world has ever seen. As the powerful figure swings along,
the massive shoulders a trifle stooped, the mighty hands
hanging listless, the eyes glazed and fixed straight ahead,
the crowd part respectfully, for he is one of the most
popular men in London. They look after him until he
disappears, and then they shake their heads regretfully
and mutter, ‘ I say, but. ain’t it a blooming shame ; poor
old Jackson.’ Down in Texas, where all the pugilists are
gathering, a sigh of relief will go up when the news comes
that Peter Jackson will never again put up his hands in
the roped arena. With the exception of his eyes and a
certain flabbiness and laxity of movement, Jackson’s
London life has left slight marks on him. Alcohol
has not swollen his face, and his cheeks have still the old,
lean, drawn look, while the high cheek - bones which
usually go with the combative temperament are as pro-
minent as ever. It is only when he talks or when he
moves around that the change in him is noticed. Some-
times when he lifts his glass his big hands shake a little,
and there is a curious halt now and then in his speech,
as if he were groping for something he could not find.
Although he has no occupation, Jackson has plenty of
money. He dresses in the very latest fashion, and from
the crown of his highly-polished silk hat to the soles of his
patent-leather shoes he is a model of correct attire.”
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —A rash paragrapliist supposes
that “ the brothers Manifold find honesty a non-paying
policy ” because their horse Apsley was heavily handi-
capped after winning four hurdle races in succession, in-
cluding a Grand National at Flemington. What nonsense !
Owners can’t get rich by losing races (although street-
corner mugs may call them clever), and when they pull
off a sequence of wins with a good animal they expect
him to be penalised accordingly. Apsley, by the way,
wasn’t crushed with “ impossible burdens” after enjoying
his picnic; for I remember him running a very close
seoond for another handicap at Flemington. Honesty is
the best policy to adopt with the best horses. Carbine
won a fortune in handicap stakes alone for Donald
Wallace, whilst a certain much “ messed-about ” star
was barely paying training expenses.—F.
“Charley ” Thompson, an alleged English “gen-
tleman ” jock., who has been warned off the turf for sus-
picious riding of his horse Bouncing Boy, is the proprietor
of a number of millinery-shops all over London, and
trades as “Madame Louise”; for which reason he is
known to his pals as “ Bonnety Ben.” He is a fine horse-
man, able to give pounds to the majority of amateurs,
and ought to have managed better than he did, for his
performance was so clumsy that one is almost inclined to
believe his defence, “ he was trying to cut it fine, and got
done by a head.” However, the disqualification of one of
the gentlemen will do a world of good (says Tup. Bulle-
tin’s correspondent), for, taking them all round, they are
about as crooked as you make ’em, and quite put pro-
fessionals in the shade for downright roguery.
» *. * *
The folly of investing on city totes is only
apparent to some of the frequently-bitten victims. A
large proportion of the entrants never start, and are
scratched on the ground. The cash planked on them in
pure ignorance usually goes into the “ runner’s ” pocket.
He pays a div. on the remainder, less commission, and if
something extra is also absorbed backers never know any-
thing about it.
One tote man runs his own little printing-office,
issuing his ’krect card, odds list, past divs., &c., daily.
A sporting Maori backed his we'Ay Carbine
against a neighbor’s unpretentious crock. Slake : a foal
each. He lost his foal. Then he offered an it.her go, this
time for a pig. He lost his pig. Allowed a concession,
he next raced horse for horse, winner taking both. He
lost his horse. One night he stole all his losses back.
Then he got three months for stealing the wrong pig.
A Sydney man, passed out lately, produced a
bookie to evidence that he backed his neddy when it
didn’t win, and didn’t when it did. As the owner had
some time previously informed a couple of the stewards
that this very ringman had “ financed ” him, they dis-
counted the testimony. The little moral is : when a
bookie happens to be your “ uncle ” keep the pawn-ticket
locked up.

Mr. Thomas P. Purves, A.1.A., for many
years manager of the New York Li e Insurance
Co. in Victoria, has now taken up Ins residence
in Sydney, on his appointment as general manager
of the co. for Australasia.
The Parkes family is widely scattered in
death. Some of the ex-Premier’s children are
buried in St. Paul’s churchyard, Birmingham
(Eng.), others in the old Sandhills cemetery, Bel-
more Road, Sydney. His first wife is in the little
plot at Faulconbridge, where also lies a sister,
whom the old man for many years maintained.
In this same plot are his son Robert and Robert’s
wife, whose orphan children the old man also
maintained, and last week by special request
Parkes’s own remains were interred in that same
spot. Parkes’s second wife, who died some months
ago, rests in the cemetery at Ryde on the Parra-
matta River—by, it is understood, her own special
request.
The first number of the new series of
Cosmos is really admirable typographically, and
the pictures make it a splendid sixpenn’orth, but
it’s to be hoped the conductors will lighten and
brighten its dull, heavy contents. There are
exceptions, of course : Louise Mack’s serial story
seems to be strengthening, though it is difficult to
judge at monthly intervals ; and one or two other
good points appear. But, on the whole, Cosmos
fails to be interesting with a large and portentous
failure. A new feature is the monthly issue to
subscribers of songs by Henri Kowalski, full
music size, for a small additional subscription.
Cecil Rhodes has been clean bowled out :
“Dr. Jim’s” raid on the Transvaal was, it has
been clearly proved, the outcome of a scheme
shared in by him, as shown by cipher cables, the
code of which was found on Jameson when he
surrendered. The Times admits Rhodes’s guilt,
but an attempt is made in the Australian press to
whitewash Csesar on the plea that it is impossible
that so careful a man as Rhodes would let himself
be caught so simply. Which is the plea advanced
on behalf of every particularly intelligent and in-
fluential thief and murderer who happens to find
himself behind the spikes.
Bertha Clair, a young woman, was charged
at Melbourne with insulting behaviour—con-
sisting in appearing in the street on Eight
Hours’ day in male attire. ‘ ‘ Why do not the
police arrest all the cyclistes in ‘ rational ’ cos-
tume?” asked Bertha’s lawyer. The P.M.
advised Miss Clair to stick to skirts for the future,
and dismissed the case. Evidently the policeman’s
view of what constitutes a male costume is in pro-
cess of revolution. A woman can now dress as
much like a man as she cares to, above the knee,
but the calf must not be disguised. Woman can
take on anything but the ordinary male bags, and
Man, poor proud Man, flatters himself that his
trousers are still his own, blind to the fact that the
time is rapidly approaching when he will be prose-
cuted for wearing ’em.
A summary of the results of S.A. general
elections: —
With the exception of Kingston, who was headed by a
friendly candidate by 6 votes, the members Of the Govt,
topped' the poll in their districts in each case by large
majorities, and whilst every member of the Labor-party
in last Assembly was re-elected, the Labor nominees were
successful in three other districts. Of the Ass party in
last Assembly Glynn, Packham, H. E. Downer, Howe,
Randell, Hawker, Riddoch and Short were badly beaten,
and one or two other Ass M’sP. were only re-elected by
small majorities. The most noteworthy defeats were
those of squatter Riddoch, displaced by jour-
nalist Morris, a strong democrat; Howe, one of the
leaders of the Ass party,' who was defeated by Roberts, a
direct Labor candidate ; and 11. E. Downer, brother of the
Opposition leader, also beaten by a Labor man, Carpenter,
As expected by The Bulletin, the Thistle crowd received
its biggest defeats in the provincial centres. S.A. pro-
ducers are beginning to see through the hypocrisy of the
ring of Adelaide monopolists who did their utmost in last
Parliament to frustrate many measui-es of genuine advan-
tage to the farmers, but the imbecile proposals of the Ass
to abolish free education and payment of members gave
that animal clean away with the country electors, who
have been crying out for local representation for years,
and who saw that non-payment meant a return to the old
system of country constituencies being ridden by metro-
politan Fat Men. Altogether the Govt, will have a work-
ing majority in the new Assembly of at least a dozen, and
asThe Democrats already have a majority in the Council,
the Kingston Ministry should be safe for another three
years.
THE SAME OLD STORY-
“Oh, it’s the same old story ; no u f e
cussing it; it’s like ‘ the everlasting 1( h ~
Rule for Ireland, Freetrade and I rotection ;
discussion never ends to the satisfaction of either .
81 ‘‘I don’t know,” quietly replied my ( companion
as he leaned over the steamers side,
question is one so momentous to tie m m
and the nation that it becomes a duty m each
to try and solve the problem !
“ People will drink—you can’t get away irom
that,” I answered.
“I don’t want to get away from human nature ,
that’s the mistake philosophers make ; but, start-
ing with the fact that people will drink, there
seems to me an easy solution of the question.
“ Indeed ! ” I smiled incredulously.
“ Yes ! to supply to people the best liquor at the
lowest price.” ,
“ If you could find some philanthropist to bear
the expense ! ”
“ I shouldn’t start it on philanthropy, but on
principles of business economy, by bringing tie
consumer into direct contact with the exporter ! ,
“ Do away with the middle-man wine-merchant s
profits ? ” I queried.
“ Precisely ! ”
“ But there can’t be any profit in that, or it
would have been done before.’
“Well, it is being done in Melbourne by a great !
London firm of wine and spirit exporters.
‘ ‘ Their name ? ” .
“ Shepherd and Co., Ltd. They have taken in
Manchester Lane fine large premises, with three
floors and about 3C,oooft. of cellar-space, probably
the largest in Melbourne. They’ve had the whole
place furnished and refitted under Salway, the
architect’s, direction. I went over the premises
the day before we left Melbourne. You should
see the cellars ; it would make your mouth water !
“ It does,” I said, drily.
“ What will you drink ? ” he laughed.
“Oh ! I’ll follow out your principles ; the best
they’ve got—say champagne ! ”
“ What brand do you like? ”
“I’ll leave that to you,” I said, not wishing to
display my merely nodding - acquaintance with
champagne.
“ Well, G. H. Mumm & Co.’s is the best! ”
“You mean you like it best? ”
“No ! not only myself. Look here ; you’ll ad-
mit the Americans understand what is good in the
matter of drinks ! ”
“ I believe so.”
“Well, I can assure you, as a fact, that accord-
ing to the statistics of champagne imported into
the United States for 1895, G. H. Mumm and Co.
are credited with nearly as much as Pommery and
Greno, Moet and Chandon, Heidseck and Co. —all
these firms put together.”
“ Let’s go below ; quick ! here’s comes my wife;
you’ll be careful ? ”
“ I understand ; ‘ Mumm’s ’ the word ! ”
“The Americans both understand what is good
and practise it,” I said, as we regained the deck.
“ They do.” His wife came along.
* * * *
Later in the day I met him. “ Come and
have a whisky with me,” I said, emphasising the
word whisky, as I couldn’t run to champagne.
“ What brand? ” I asked, as one asks of an expert.
“John Robertson’s, ” he said, and his answer made
me glad. I’ve always drunk J.R.D., and I was
pleased to know on his authority I had not been
wasting good drinking-time.
“ Other whiskies may come and go,” he said
sagely, “ but John Robertson’s stands the test of
time and competition.”
That night, over coffee, he introduced me to a
glass of delicious cognac brandy, and I wrote
down for future reference the name of the brand,
“A. C. Meukhow & Co.” Seeing I was interested
in drink, generally and specifically, he returned to
the subject of Shepherd and Co.’s new enterprise.
“ There’s one thing they’ve decided firmly on,
and that is that they will not touch colonial dis-
tillations at all. If their customers want Colonial
spirits they must get them elsewhere. When you
go back to Melbourne you should call on the firm
and see thoir premises. One fact that may interest
you as a Victorian is that they import all their
spirits in the wood, and the bottling, labelling,
and casing will distribute about £lO,OOO a-year
among Melbourne artisans and general workers.”
“Shepherd and C 0.,” he proceeded, “ as they
are direct representatives here, for their own sakes
can only afford to take such firms as have estab-
lished reputations— e.g., their ports and sherries
they receive from Sandeman, Sons and Co. San-
deman, the head of this firm, is a present Governor
of the Bank of England. Their pure Jamaica
rum comes from Lamb and Sons, and their un-
sweetened gin from Vickers and Co., distillers to
the Queen. They do not bottle their Guinness’
stout here; that is done in London by Daukes
and Co. ; and Heineken’s Lager beer (Pilsener
and Bavarian) is imported bottled. They keep in
their cellars, too, Scotia’s Pride (Bruce Bros and
Co.), O’Grady’s Old Irish (Dublin) whisky' and
Don Carlos port and sherry.”
“ I should like to pay them a visit of about a
week s duration !
“You’ll find it worth your while, and they’ll be
pleased to see you.”
“I suppose it took some time to start such a
business?
“Not long ! The gentleman who is now the
manager of the company in Melbourne left for
Englandtocxmfer with his principals on 7th Sept
1890, and after six weeks in England and on the
Continent, where he came into contact with all
the great distillers and bottlers, he completed his
export arrangements and reached Melbourne on
mh Dec ’95. On 20th Jan., ’96, the business
was launched.

From the English Chukch Times of 21st
Feb., 189 C
APPEAL. -Will anyone, of their charity, give a help
towards getting a small IRON CHAPEL to seat 70, for a
poor community who have lost their all for a permanent
church through the failure of the Australian banks ?
Apply to House of Mercy, Joppa, Edinburgh.
The accident whereby a young girl was
killed through the snapping of a davit on board a
Sydney excursion steamer, the other day, calls to
mind a similar case at a Scotch watering-place a
few years ago. As usual, a couple of officious
passengers took the place of the deck-hands when
lowering the boat, which was let down at a terrific
pace, giving the occupants a ducking and severely
maiming two children who were unconcernedly
looking over the side on the lower deck. At the
subsequent investigation the action of the pas-
sengers was considered censurable, but the s.s.
Co. was held fully responsible and mulct in heavy
damages. The co. disclaimed all liability for a
traveller’s action ; but, on being asked if they
Would not prevent a passenger taking hold of the
wheel, they, of course, replied in the affirmative,
which gave their case away. A rope is as much
part of a ship as a wheel.
The following, taken from Otago Witness
(M.L.), is one of the reasons why The Bulletin
votes for cremation :
A little above the south end of the Avenue of Foxton
sits upon the grassed slopes of the sandhills the small
cemetery of the village. Inside that enclosure is another
little blue and white one, and down under the flowers
growing between the sea shells strewn there lies in an
everlasting sleep all that was once Flo. Marie, for the tiny
marble up here saj's : “In loving memory of Florence
Marie May, who joined the angels to day.” Then the
date follows, and after that the touching intimation,
“ Jesus loves me.” Flo. Marie died a bridesmaid, and
this was the tale : The wedding-party—bride and brides-
maid (Flo. Marie), bridegroom and groomsman, parson
and friends and all—were there. The ceremony over,
bride and bridegroom kissed the smiling bridesmaid lean-
ing against a wall ; but it wasn’t Flo. Marie it was only
her clay. The Reaper had come and smiled on the
floweret. This is death. That was many years ago.
Any kind of change which will prevent the public
maundering over the tomb of Little Eva and such
things must be a change for the better. The
trouble doesn’t lie so much in the weeping of the
deceased’s relatives ; it is the unauthorised person
who insists on snuffling sadly over other people
whom he never saw before, simply because they
are buried, that is objectionable.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
quyer of all other Australian gems.*
Truth again distancing fiction. In the
Lebaudy scandal, France, evidence is given that a
consumptive girl was brought from Marseilles to
kill Lebaudy, and she is produced before the tri-
bunal.

Dear Sir, —It gives me great pleasure to inform
you that I have derived great benefit from your
celebrated Alfaline Remedies during my explor-
ing expeditions throughout Australia, and I con-
sider them invaluable to all travellers in the bush.

Witness : A. F. Bekkr, Manager, Grand Hotel,
Spring-street, Melbourne.
Mr. Button writes later :—“ The Pills I had
from you were of great benefit to me and others.
In one case, when three hunters and trappers
were travelling about the ranges near my place on
the Rose River, one was taken very ill; his mates
intended taking him in a waggon to Wangaratta
(about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor. I
gave him a few of your Pills. lam pleased to
inform you that having to pass their camp next
day, I found him up and pegging-out his skins
and looking well. This speaks volumes for your
valuable Remedies.”

Dear Sir, —In the year of 18711 was eight days
in the water sheep-washing, and contracted rheu-
matism and stiffness in the joints, and have tried
all advertised medicines. When I came to Sydney
I had a great difficulty in walking, and went to
the Turkish baths, which did no good. Passing
your window I saw a testimonial from Mr. E.
W. O’Sullivan, M.P., with the same complaint as
I had, which led me to try your Remedies. On
Thursday before Good Friday I took the first of
your Pills; since then I have used 4 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Vitality Pills, 3 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Rheumatic Pills, with Liver
and Kidney Pills, and free use of your Alfaline
Eucalyptus Oil, and I am now, with the blessing
of God, as well in health as I was 23 years ago.
I can with great confidence recommend your
Remedies to anyone suffering from the same com-
plaint. May you long be spared under God to
be a blessing to suffering humanity.
I remain, dear sir, yours very gratefully,
JOHN KEAY.

Henry Lawgon’s Poems.—Post-free from
.bulletin office, ss. sd. in stamps or postal notes
ol any Australasian province.
A new thing in mouse-shifting and rat-
hustling is a phosphorescent terra-cotta cat, which
gleams grimly just by the rat or mouse-hole, and
ights up the pantry wondrously and fearsomely.
-Also, it induces sobriety on lodge-nights.
R.A. President Leighton is said to have
left no property beyond his furniture and effects,
even if his household goods are not encroached
upon by his liabilities. He was not a prolific
painter, and he lived high.
There is not much sympathy with Italy
amongst the strong Roman Catholics, who see the
hand of God in the late defeat of the Italians, and
lay stress upon the fact that the Abyssinian forces
were actually armed with old Papal weapons
which had been sold to them by the Italians
themselves.
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block m Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books ; 2, Second-

John Grice distinguished himself on the
question of lady resident practitioners at Melb.
Hospital. Grice thought that the feelings of the
patients ought to be considered ; “ moreover, many
of them were suffering from diseases which, to say
the least, it would be very hard for lady doctors to
illustrate before a mixed class of pupils of the
same age as themselves.” Good Griceous ! Every
third patient in the Hospital is a woman, and, if
another woman can’t illustrate Tier case to a mixed
class with more propriety than a young man can
do the same thing, The Bulletin would like to
know the reason why.

E. B. Loughran, a Yic. poet who recently
issued a volume of verse and failed to ignite the
Yarra, discovers that he experienced a calamity in
having been born a poet. The successes of ‘ ‘ Banjo ”
and Lawson trouble him muchly. “ If,” he wails,
“ with an ear for jingle the rhymster combines the
slany of horse-racing with some knowledge of the
shady side of bush life, and has digested his Kip-
ling to the point of assimilation, he may produce a
book of verse that will run into three editions, but
if he has ‘ the vision and the faculty divine,’ if he
has seen ‘ the light that never was on land or sea,’
and attempts to deliver a message, then ”
well then he suffers neglect, like Kendall, Gordon
—and Loughran. If Mr. Loughran’s compara-
tive failure had not induced envy and un-
charitableness, he would admit that the bards he
inferentially contemns had made their marks be-
fore ever Kipling appeared above the horizon. But
much criticism of Australian stuff in Australia has
not yet risen superior to the bald insinuation con-
tained in an allusion to Bret Harte, or Swinburne,
or Kipling. It is not even counted to the verse-
maker’s credit that he imitated Kipling before
Kipling was.

Oddment posted on the Melb. telegraph-
posts last week: “If men and women would
banish beer, gambling, and other vices, and save
their badly-spent money to defeat their employers,
they could rule the labor-market and swamp the
unemployed difficulty. They might become wives
and husbands, instead of prostitutes and bludgers.
—Yours truly, A Simple Girl.”
“ A plague of flies and mosquitoes is ravag-
ing the Northern Territory,” writes a Bulletin
correspondent. “They follow cattle in millions,
and drive them into frenzy. The other day a bush-
man had one of his horses killed by the pests,
and fearing to lose the other, covered it with
a coating of thin paint to which a little carbolic
had been added. This device was completely suc-
cessful.” Was it? It ought to have killed the
horse.

A Case for the Psychologist,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In this age of the neurotic and erotic female, it
is generally a woman’s own fault if she finds her-
self in weird situations; yet it may occasionally
happen that an innocent and sensitive girl un-
avoidably undergoes a cruel embarrassment such
as only fellow women can appreciate—or old
doctors like myself. Let me put it into proper
story-form for you. She told me—she had to tell
somebody.
It was her first day up, and she was lying on a
couch in the drawing-room, where the sunlight,
mellowed by its passage through the yellow art-
muslin curtains, touched her here and there with
vague spots of light. A mere girl in appearance
(though worn with recent illness), she was yet
looking back on five years of married life. Such
happy years—she was thinking in that acutely-
emotional frame of mind that comes with the
weakness of convalescence. From the very first
he had changed the world for her. On the evening
she had acknowledged his bow of introduction,
the reserved, timid girl had given her heart to the
bright, alert young fellow with the cheery smile
and frank, pleasant ways—given it with a secret
shame and self-reproach which the matron of five
years’ standing could almost smile to recall. But
all that was over now, over like the terrible illness
through which she had passed—happy wife, happy
mother, listening for her husband’s foot upon the
threshold . . . and Ethel, her little four-year child
—was that her voice singing among the laurel
bushes beyond the verandah ?
The yellow curtains swelled inward, moved by
the gently-rising breeze; she was falling into a
doze again when there was a sound of voices.
“ She is on the sofa. You can go in. I’ll be
with you presently.”
The door opened.
She awoke with a start. “ How long you’ve
been, dear; I’ve had such a nice sleep ! Are
those pansies for me? Yes, lay them on the
table ; mother will bring in a bowl for them pre-
sently.” She pushed back the hair from her fore-
head and looked up at him smilingly. “ How
nice you look, Frank ! I’m afraid it will be a
long time before I get my good looks back again.
Poor, dear fellow,” and she stroked his cheek
pityingly, “it would be an awful shame for a
beauty-loving man like you to be saddled with
an ugly wife.”
“ Yes—no—it doesn’t matter ! ”
“ Doesn’t it ? Well, you needn’t get so red
about it. Can’t you sit still with me a little
while ! Where are you going ? ”
“ I thought—perhaps you’d like to see your
mother.” And through his mind flashed : “ I must
take care what I say; she couldn’t bear a shock
just now.”
“Oh ! thank you,” with playful petulance ;
“ I’ve had mother all morning ; I should like a
little of my husband’s society just for a change.
How do you think the old home’s looking ? Not
so nice as our own little house, of course, but the
country air will be so good for Ethel after the heat
of Sydney. Do you know, Frank, I can never
understand how we came to have such a handsome
child ; neither of us is very good-looking, you
know—at least you aren’t; yet there she is the
frettiest little girl in Sydney. Other people say so;
didn’t.”
Her quick mood changed from playfulness to
pathos. “ I’ve been thinking of the days we first
met ; she was lying back once more, her hands
beneath her head, looking up at him with that
perfect sense of possession that scorns the added
assurance of touch or caress ; “ you’ve been very
kind to me—all these years. I was fond of my
father and mother, but my love for you is quite
different.”
A movement at the door. Her gaze had
wandered as she spoke the last few words; now
she raised herself abruptly in her astonishment:
he had stolen softly from her side, and was stand-
ln§[ by the door, his hand raised to open it.
Her cheek flushed. “Frank,” she said, pite-
o’isly; “ why are you leaving me like that?” . . .
How strange you look ! ”
She sprang from the sofa, and, tottering in her
weakness, stood holding fast to the back of a chair.
“ Don’t—don’t look like that.” A quick pallor
overspread her features ; she was conscious of a
sick despair, of a striving to grasp something—un-
derstand something— what, she did not know. Then
she felt his arm round her, and with a sigh of
relief, even a faint smile, she sank back on the
sofa—sitting, this time, her head against the wall.
"No wonder you looked strange, Frank. Why,
I’ve actually been thinking of Ethel as a little
girl, when she’s only a baby—a little baby in long
clothes. It must be the effect of my illness, I
suppose !”
“ Yesj of course”—hurriedly; “now, sit still,
while I call your mother ! ”
“ ‘ Sit—-still—while—I—call—your— mother.’
Frank ! ” She pressed her hands to her forehead,
and rose slowly to her feet, looking not at him,
but at the wall opposite. “ Why am I repeating
your _ words like this ? Oh ! there’s something
slipping away from me! Save me, Frank, don’t
let it—my mind’s travelling backwards, back-
wards.”
“Oh !” in a tone of piercing horror : “ I never
had a child. . . . Oh, my God, what have I
been saying to you ? . . . You are Mr. Frank
Allison, and I have met you only once before ! ”
“That’s all. Curious, isn’t it? Given a sudden
love-emotion, followed by a severe illness with its
accompaniment of delirious dreaming, and there
you have the anticipations of the future tem-
porarily assuming the reality of the past.”
“ But, look here, how did it end ? Surely he
had the decency to fall in love with her after
that. ”
“ My dear fellow, he had the hardest job to win
her after that. The purest woman can’t easily
endure the idea that her forward-looking thoughts
have been self-revealed to the man she loves,
especially before he has declared himself.”
“ But how did it end ? ”
“ Well, I saw her just the other day. She has
everything now —the loving husband, the little
house in town, the annual visit to her mother’s
that ‘ Ethel ’ may enjoy the fresh air, only .”
“ Only what ? ”
The old Doctor looked up with a twinkle in his
eye.
“ One thing went wrong. ‘ Ethel ’is a boy ! ”
Maoriland,
C. CLYDE.
A recent suicide at Hawera (M.L.) is a
whole essay on the illogicalness of woman. The
deceased, who was aged 17, consulted a fortune-
teller, who told her she would die when between
19 and 20. The prospect of dying two years hence
preyed on her mind so much that she went and
died at once; or rather, she killed herself to
avoid death.
The "Provisional School,"
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was built in the “humpy” style of
architecture—sapling frame-work covered with
stringy-bark—and stood on a rather steep siding
where the earth had been dug away to give the
building a chance of holding on. The “ face ” of
the excavation was level with the eaves, and a
boy might have stepped from the edge on to the
log weights of the roof. There was but one room,
about 24 x 16 feet, and a little “skillion ” below
the surface at the back for the accommodation of
the master, who was a bachelor then. Seen along
the “siding,” the humpy seemed to be leaning
well into the hillside, as it afraid of slipping down
into the main road and being run over by the
traffic. This hut was called a “ provisional ”
school, and we connected the word vaguely with
tucker.
* * * *
One of the master’s pet notions was that
the hut.might be blown down at any moment in a
high wind—greatly to the anger and disgust of
the bushman who built it—and he trained the
scholars to dive under the desks at a given signal,
so that, in case the worst happened, they might
escape the falling rafters. We were privately
resolved to dive for the door at the first warning
crack.
* * * *
The fittings consisted of part of the worn-
out furniture of a neighboring town school. The
books came second-hand from the same school.
They had been, according to the title-page, “ pub-
lished for use in the public schools of Ireland,”
and were mostly about Ireland. The “master”
was Irish, too. We had one “geography,” which
hinted vaguely of a large unexplored continent
called New Holland, and said: “ The inhabitants
of New Holland are amongst the lowest and most
degraded to be found on the surface of the earth.”
We remember that this sentence always impressed
us greatly when he ran against it —because of the
roll of it, no doubt. Furthermore, the volume
stated that some of the poorer inhabitants of
Norway made a kind of bread out of the inner
bark of a (sort of tree) —which so exasperated a
certain hairy ex-viking ; who had settled there,
that he withdrew his children from the school and
taught them at home.
The book contained several other cheerful and
interesting statements, which the children never
doubted. “It’s in the book,” they said ; and that
clinched it. We hadn’t begun to doubt the news-
papers then. The reading books, with moral little
stories—with the scenes laid in the vicinity of
Athlone—about the small and very ragged
children of extremely poor and doggedly
honest parents, and ladies at the “great house,”
and “the squire” who occasionally distributed
pennies ; and the moral of most of them was that
it is better to be ragged and starved and in danger
of being run in for vagrancy than to be rich and
discontented. We believed it all then—but we
doubt now. We believed in and took for granted
many things which we don’t believe now. Ah,
for the faith of childhood! The master was
always right then, the elders of the district were
right, and print was infallible.
* * * *
A big, easy-going iguana had his head-
quarters in the hollow of a tree near the school-
house. We called him “Stripes,” because of the
black and yellow rings round his tail. He might
have been a “she,” but “he” seemed to fit him
better. We always oalled an animal “he ” when
doubtful of the gender. “ Stripes ” was under the
master’s protection, and well the wily lizard knew
it. He’d camp about the “ rislers ” and logs of
the roof on sunny days; and sometimes, during
school-hours, he’d come in through a hole in the
bark and He along on a tie-beam over the girls’
seat, and think and doze, and improve his mind,
or refresh it, maybe—for he looked as if his educa-
tion had begun before man started his—and blink
at the master, and at last be droned fast to sleep
by the children’s voices.
And sometimes one of the boys would watch his
chanoe to whisper hoarsely :
“ Gur-r-rls ! —the gohanna’s failin’ 1 ”
Then you’d hear the girls squawk.
* * * *
There has been a brick public school there
for many years, and masters of the new school, and
new systems.
H.L.
Says Sue, in Thos. Hardy's “Jude the
Obscure ” : —“ I have no fear of men, as such, nor
of their books. I have mixed with them—one or
two of them particularly—almost as one of their
own sex. I mean I have not felt about them as
most women are taught to feel—to be on their
guard against attacks on their virtue; for no
average man—no man short of a sensual savage—
will molest a woman by day or night, at home or
abroad, unless she invites him. Until she says by
a look ‘ come on ’ he is always afraid to, and if
you never say it, or look it, he never comes.”
“You and your comrades made me feel
that Australasia is one in its devotion to the in-
terests of the Army—the salvation of the world,
loyalty to the principles of the Army, and affection
for the ‘ General. ’ May it continue and increase
until we meet before the Throne. ” —Booth’s letter
to the Harmy leader in Melbourne. They all
seem to take it for granted that the Almighty has
a throne— also a crown and sceptre. The religious
conception of a King of Kings breathes the most
vulgar spirit of worldly grovel. No wonder Queen
Victoria is Defender of the Faith, and bishops sit
in the House of Lords, and Albert Edward pre-
tends to say his prayers. Good old faith !
Jameson’s downfall, over the inglorious
Transvaal filibustering expedition, has proved a
blessing in disguise to the British S. Africa Co.,
whose affairs he administered locally, and the very
best thing that could have happened to ensure the
co. ’s safety in Matabeleland. After the defeat and
capture of the little Scotch medico and his forces
at Kriigersdorp, the Chartered Co., fearing an
attack on its territory by the Boers in retaliation
for “Dr. Jim’s” impudent aggression, sent out
secret agents to enlist soldiers for the defence of
Buluwayo. These agents travelled all over the
Cape and S. Africa, and succeeded in gathering
together a fine body of ex-soldiers, who were paid
at the rate of 12s. 6d. per day and promised £1
per diem when on active service. Hundreds of
able-bodied men were in this way quietly smuggled
into Buluwayo, and, after being equipped, dis-
tributed all over the co.’s territory. The Char-
tered Co., whose regular forces had been disbanded
after the slaughter of Lo Bengula’s warriors, was
thus well prepared for attack —a circumstance for
which it has solely to thank Jameson’s downfall.
The bolt, however, came from where it was least
expected. The Matabeles “revolted,” and the
Boers, for whose reception elaborate systems of
defence had been undertaken, magnanimously
offered to send help to the co. in order to protect
the women and children in laager at Buluwayo.

At Night.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The night is young yet : an enchanted night
In early Summer —soft, calm, cool, and bright.
The moonlight fills the sleeping valley up,
Like white wine poured into a earven cup.
I love the Night! I love each little breeze
She brings to charm the sleep of dreaming trees.
I like her Moon, the theme of countless rhymes
(I’ve written some of these, myself, at times).
The Night has noises. Sough! Susurrus! Hark
’Tis Mother Nature whispering in the dark.
Burden of cities, and turmoil of men,
Joys, griefs, unmeaning—she forgets them then.
Her breasts are bare; to Grief she brings surcease
She gives her restless sons the Milk of Peace ;
Lulls them to sleep ; draws them from thoughts oi
pelf ;
Telling them sweet, old stories of herself.
Ah, me ! If I could but contrive, to write
These stories—half of them—in black and white,
The sons of men would throng from ev’ry land
Beneath the sun to kiss this simple hand ;
And they would bring me gifts a king might prize,
And fill my rooms with rarest merchandise.
The starkest Philistine, uncouthest Goth,
Would clothe my limbs in costly suits of cloth ;
And Latakia I should smoke, indeed,
Instead of this weird, black, Australian weed ;
And ev’ry soul of them would clamor, “ Mine
The glory, first, to fill his cup with Wine ! ”
All secrets deep, all that I hear and see
Of hidden things, the Night reveals to me.
If I could only tell the things I know,
And feel, and hear ! Pate will not have it so.
Yet will I try to lightly indicate
Their nature —with apologies to Fate !
I know what ev’ry flow’r with drowsy head
Down-drooping drfeams of—and the seeming-dead,
I know how they, escaped from care and strife,
Ironically moralise on Life ;
And know what—when the moon walks on the
waves —
They whisper to each other in their graves.
I know that white clouds, drifting from stark
coasts
Across the sky at midnight, are the ghosts
Of sailors drowned at sea, who yearn to win
A quiet grave beside their kith and kin,
In still green churchyards, where these lie at ease,
Far from the sound of surge, and roar of seas.
I know the message of the mournful rain
That beats upon the widow’s window-pane ;
It says “ God’s mercy with God’s sorrow goes,”
And deeper things—but these God only knows.
I know the meaning of the roar of seas ;
I know the glad spring sap-song of the trees,
And that great chant to which, in tuneful grooves,
The green round Earth upon its axis moves,
And that still greater chant the Bright Sun sing* —
Fire-crowned Apollo—the great chant that bring*
All things to life, and draws through spaces dim,
And star-sown realms, his planets after him.
I know the tune that led, since Life began,
The upward, downward, onward March of Man.
I hear the whispers that the Arngels twain,
Of Death and Life, exchange in meeting. Fain
Are they to pause ; their errands will not stay—
“ Never ! ” “ For ever ! ” This is all they say.
I hear the twitterings, inarticulate,
Of souls unborn that press around the gate
Of Birth, each striving which shall first escape
From formless vapor into human shape ;
And see how mostly, in that struggle blind,
The meanest souls the fairest bodies find.
I know the tale the bird of passionate heart,
The Nightingale, tries ever to impart
To men, though vainly—for I well believe
That in her brown breast beats the heart of Eve,
Who, with her sweet, sad, wistful music, tries
To tell her sons of their lost Paradise,
And solemn secrets man had grace to know,
When God walked in the garden, long ago.
Yea, I have seen, with trembling and with awe
The vision terrible Lucretius saw :
The great white Universe—suns, stars, grief, bliss—
Plunging for ever down a black abyss.
But give me Taylor—Bishop Jeremy—
Who likens all the star-worlds that we see
(Which seem to run an everlasting race)
Unto a snowstorm sweeping on through space . . .
Suns, planets, stars : in glorious array
They march upon their unknown, wondrous way.
Thought, seraph-wing’d, and swifter than the light,
Unto the dim verge of the Infinite
Pursues them, through that strange ethereal flood
In which they swim—(mayhap it is the blood
G-f Universal God, wherein they are
But corpuscles ; sun, asteroid, and star ;
And this great starry stream we see, a dim
Small pulse in'the remotest vein of Him) —
Pursues in vain, and from lone, awful glooms
Turns back to earth again with weary plumes.
Well! let them go! They know their own affairs—
Ours, after all, may be as great as theirs.
Great, small, swift, slow are terms. Full oft a vain
Young thought grows grey in passing through the
brain.
Yet that which is lives while it lives : so I.
Give me the earth ; the saints may have the sky.
Through glacial gulfs of space the soul must roam
To know the comfort of this earthly home.
Ah, Mother dear ! Broad-bosomed Mother Earth !
Mother of all our Joy, Grief, Madness, Mirth !
Mother of Flow’r and Fruit, of Stream and Sea
We are thy children and we cling to thee.
I lay my head upon thy breast, and hear,
Small, small and faint, yet strangely sweet and
clear,
The hum and clash of little worlds below,
Each on its own path moving, swift or slow j
And list’ning, ever, with intenter ear,
Through din of wars invisible, I hear
A Homer—genius is not gauged by mass
Singing his Iliad on a blade of grass.
And nations hearken ; his great song resounds
Unto the tussock’s very utmost bounds.
States rise and fall, each blade of grass upon,
But still his song, from blade to blade, rolls on
Through all the tussock-world ; and Helen still
Is fairest fair, and Ajax, wild of will —
An Ajax whose huge length when measured o er
Is full ten-thousandth of an inch, or more —
Still hurls defiance at his gods, whose home
Is in the distant, awful, dew-drop dome
That trembling hangs, suspended from a spray,
An inch above him—worlds of space away.
Old prophecies foretell, but Time proves all,
The day will come when it, like Troy, shall fall. . .
Lo ! Through this small, great wondrous song
there runs
The marching-melody of stars and suns !
At times I hear, in God’s great Loom of Life,
The shuttles sing of Joy, Woe, Peace and Strife,
And (dreamwise) see Him bright with dark threads
twist—
The grand, old, archetypal Humorist.
I know these things, yet cannot others tell
Their meanings. Over all is cast a spell.
Secrets they are, sealed with a seven-fold seal,
My soul knows what my tongue may not reveal.
I love the Night! De Musset wrote at night
His Orient-perfumed poems exquisite,
His Nights of Cleopatra, and could make
Wax-dips the place of bright Canopus take.
Balzac, by lamplight, with black-magic art,
Exposed the mysteries of woman’s heart.
Woman ! The lines I’ve written I have scanned—
They hardly mention you. I kiss your hand !
I kiss your hand collective ! Ah, could I
An Ugly Woman see before I die—
One, only, Ugly Woman ! I would frame
A song of her would set men’s hearts aflame.
But she is dead, and useless are complaints—
Sweet Ugly Woman buried with the saints !
I love the Night! Bright Day the soul shuts in ;
Night sends it soaring to its starry kin.
If I must leave at last my place of birth,
This homely, gracious, green, familiar Earth,
With all it holds of sorrow and delight,
I pray my parting-hour may be at Night,
And that her curtain dark may softly fall
On scenes I love, ere I depart from all.
Then shall I, haply, journeying through the Vast
Mysterious Silences, take one, long, last,
Fond look at Earth, and watch, from depths afar,
I he dear old planet dwindling to a star,
And sigh, “Farewell!” unto the friends of yore,
Whose kindly faces I shall see no more.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
Westralians would like to be assured of
the value of the harbour improvement scheme at
Fremantle, on which a lot of money has been sunk.
The work comprises the prolongation of the Swan
River mouth by two miles, the removal of a rocky
bar, deepening of the channel to 30ft., and some
reclamation and many incidentals. This will
make a very small harbour, and the Swan River
anyway has only a 13ft. channel to Perth. Admiral
Bridge, when visiting Fremantle, refused to ex-
press any opinion of the value of the work, which
is considered ominous. It is not yet demonstrated
that the province will get value for the £BOO,OOO
which the job will cost, and the sum anyway is a
lot of money for West Australia to spend on
doubtful improvements to the harbour.
This year’s Melb. Photographic Exhibition
shows a vast improvement over last year’s similar
show. The past 12 months have evidently been
devoted by Australian amateurs to the study
of pictorial effect as well as to the development of
technical skill, and as a result they have scored
heavily. The landscapes, both in choice of sub-
ject, printing and tone, are in nearly every case
exquisite examples of photographic art, and com-
pare favorably with any productions of the old
world. In architecture the same high standard is
maintained, the majority following the effective
Yankee method of showing a building- in the
bright, midday sun with the lights and shadow*
in strong contrast, instead of the English fashion
of exhibiting a dull drab surface with a clouded
sky. One picture in particular, that of Melb.
Town Hall portico, is worthy to rank with the
best reproductions of Habper’s or the Century.
Another branch entered on for the first time is
that out of which Downey, of London, makes a
huge income—the representation of handsome
female figures in the centre of some striking land-
scape, after the style of Marcus Stone’s pictures.
These are all in the first class, but where the nude
has been attempted the one miserable failure of
the whole exhibition is manifest. None of the
photographs have been happy in ch ice of models,
all those exhibited being distinctly . ihandsome in
feature and very faulty in bodily .- nmetry; yet
it is not impossible to obtain good .unale models
as proved by last year’s exhibition, where a Venus-
like girl’s form was utterly wasted by bad posing.
The poses this year are also very weak—academic
and artificial, and with no freedom or action about
them ; and none of the artists appear to recognise
JP® difference between nudity and nakedness.
Nothing is more chaste than the nude,’’says Taffy,
m ‘ ‘ Trilby ; ” but these women are not nude, but
naked, and, as such, are offensive to all but the
prurient eye. To accentuate the idea of undress
most of them have some drapery or other clumsily
introduced as a sop to Respectability, which
defeats its own end. Ambitious photographers of
the nude would do well to purchase “LeNudu
oa-lon, and study the effects of posing, or if
unable to assimilate any of its spirit, to copy the
poses outright. The one exception to the undressed
horror is that of a nude child climbing a cliff. It

Tb« NEW “LIP-FLAN6E” FRUE VANNER BELT.
“ Will Outlast Two of tbc Ordsoary.”
Genuine “ Frue ” Belts (obtainable only from
the patentees or their authorised agents) have, owing
to the great care and attention paid to their manu-
facture, invariably given good satisfaction, but, after
being in use a long time, the old flange had a ten-
dency to become weak and lose its vertical position.
This prevented the machine carrying the proper depth
of load, and allowed the sand to slop over and get
into the bearings. The new “ Lip-Flange,” which
SECTIONAL VIEW OF FLANGE
has just been patented throughout the colonies, does
away with this difficulty almost entirely. It is
made of a very superior and elastic rubber prepared
in a special manner, and its peculiar shape prevents
it cracking in going over the rollers, or losing its
vertical position. All “ Prue Yanners ” sold in the
future will be fitted with this new “ Lip-Flange ”
Belt without additional charge, and the belts them-
selves may be procured from the patentees at a very
slight advance on the old price.
